D. THE BORROWER'S OBJECTIVES AND POLICIES
1.20    The Borrower's Priorities and Objectives: Regarding drainage, the Borrower's short-term
objectives and priorities articulated in the Eighth Five-Year Plan (FY93/94-97/98) are to accelerate the
pace of implementation of ongoing projects, protect borderline waterlogged areas through preventive
measures, limit public sector investment in subsurface drainage to SGW areas, and strengthen the linkages
between drainage research and development. Other short-term objectives and priorities held in common
with the irrigation subsector are to increase the role of the private sector in development and maintenance
of water sector infrastructure, pilot-test the concept of autonomous organizations for O&M, and intensify
post-completion monitoring and evaluation (M&E). In the long term, the Borrower's priorities for
drainage include measures to: (i) reduce the drainable surplus through improved water management,
reducing or intercepting seepage at source, and biological drainage"4; and (ii) exploit the residual potential
of adversely affected resources, by such means as saline agriculture. These objectives reflect the
recommendations of the National Conservation Strategy (NCS) adopted by the Borrower in 1992". To
control waterlogging and salinity, the strategy advocates an integrated drainage approach, i.e., a
combination of, among other things, intensive cropping and tree planting, promotion of salt-tolerant crops
and cropping systems, provision of gypsum for salt-affected areas (gypsum is a soil amendment that
facilitates the reclamation of certain salt-affected soils), installation of tubewells, construction of surface
drains by farmers, and provision of subsurface drainage for selected areas. The NCS also highlights the
urgency of implementing a Wetlands Management Plan.
1.21    The Policy Framework and Drainage Sector Environmental Assessment (DSEA)"6: The
Borrower and IDA commissioned the DSEA in response to the perceived deterioration of the drainage and
environmental status of the IBIS. The DSEA was financed by a grant from the United Nations
Development Program's (UNDP) Umbrella Trust Fund, managed by the Bank, and was completed and
published in June 1993. It makes strategy recommendations for the medium term that have been adopted
by the Borrower as its strategy. DSEA's strategy is based on the following principles: (i) no project that
mobilizes salts from deep groundwater aquifers should be undertaken unless such salts can be safely
disposed of in the sea; (ii) additional irrigation water, if available, should not be supplied to areas where
this could exacerbate the need for drainage; (iii) the Government should not provide structural drainage
interventions that can reasonably be carried out by the private sector; (iv) the Government should confine
its subsurface drainage interventions to SGW areas where there is an environmentally acceptable solution
for the disposal of drainage effluent; (v) the Government should concentrate its surface drainage
interventions in areas where it is justified by the risk of storm water damage or the irrigation process, or
where it is linked to private sector drainage or subsurface drainage in SGW areas; and (vi) the Government
should give special attention to beneficiary participation in structural and non-structural interventions.
1.22   Implementation: The Borrower is implementing most recommendations of the DSEA by: (i)
continuing the ongoing program to phase out government tubewells in FGW areas; (ii) replacing the
existing SCARP Tubewells (STWs) in SGW areas with tile drains wherever possible, with beneficiaries
assuming responsibility for O&M; (iii) installing tile drains where possible for new drainage development
in SGW areas. However, the facilities would be provided only where there are safe options for disposal of
the effluent, and only where the beneficiaries agree to be involved in the construction and O&M of the
outlets to watercourses are constructed and maintained by PIDs. Watercourses and field channels are operated and
maintained by farmers.
14     Certain tree species are able to draw down the water table in their immediate surroundings; they therefore
function as 'biological' pumps.
15     The Borrower prepared the National Conservation Strategy in 1991 with the assistance of the Canadian
International Development Agency (CIDA) and the United Nations Development Program (UNDP).
16     Government of Pakistan: Drainage Sector Environmental Assessment (DSEA), June 1993.



8
facilities; (iv) continuing support for controlling groundwater levels in SGW areas through sharing
investment and O&M costs with the beneficiaries at appropriate levels because of externalities; (v)
encouraging construction of tertiary (on-farm) drains by the beneficiaries; (vi) directing investments in
main and secondary surface drains to areas where crop damage as a result of storm runoff can be reduced,
groundwater tables are rising, and sufficient irrigation supplies are available to cultivate reclaimed land,
and where disposal drains are required for saline effluent of subsurface drainage facilities; and (vii)
undertaking major drainage schemes in conjunction with preventive measures at source, i.e., improvement
of irrigation management at main and secondary levels and reduction of water losses at watercourse and
field levels.
1.23    The Borrower's NDP (1995-2020): The Borrower has formulated a 25 year NDP (1995 to
2020), with an estimated cost of Rs. 90 billion (1993 prices) for 77 projects; of which Rs. 18 billion had
been spent by 1993. By the completion date of the program, the Borrower expects to provide the necessary
drainage facilities for the entire Indus Basin.
1.24    The National Surface Drainage System (NSDS): The planners of Pakistan's IBIS and its
principal donors led by IDA always knew that ultimately, the Indus Basin Irrigation System would require
an outlet to the sea. The consultants who carried out the DSEA were required inter alia to evaluate various
disposal options to deal with this alarming problem of salinity i.e., other than constructing the outlet to the
sea; they concluded that it was neither avoidable nor postponable. Sustainability can only be achieved in
the long run by building a NSDS. Given the long lead time required for detailed planning, feasibility
studies, and inter-provincial agreement on cost sharing and transportation of saline effluent from the Upper
Bank (and possibly Right Bank) Indus to the sea via Sindh Province, work on the NSDS is probably
already overdue.
1.25   Institutional Reforms: Within the framework of this project, a package of major reforms which
implement all significant aspects of the strategy has been agreed. Most of these reforms are reflected in the
Borrower's Sector Development Policy Letters submitted to IDA, dated August 21, 1995 and October 3,
1995. The reforms consist primarily of decentralization and management transfer of the irrigation and
drainage system from PIDs to a multi-tier system of autonomous institutions with clearly defined roles and
responsibilities within the system; and with a firm commitment to phase out subsidies for O&M in seven to
ten years. The hierarchy of institutions and their roles and responsibilities are sumsmarized below: (i) the
role of WAPDA's Water Wing would eventually be limited to inter-Provincial functions; (ii) PIDs would
be converted into autonomous Provincial Irrigation and Drainage Authorities (PIDAs), with responsibility
for the intra-Provincial aspects of the system from barrages to canal headworks, and from main drains that
cross canal commands and major drainage basins to inter-Provincial drains operated and maintained by
WAPDA; (iii) self-accounting Area Water Boards (AWBs), initially set up as pilots, would be established
around selected canal commands to take over and manage the irrigation and drainage system from canal
headworks to distributaries/minors operated by FOs, and from the branch drains operated by FOs to main
drains operated by PIDAs; and (iv) FOs owned and controlled by farmers would also be encouraged
through a series of pilots in each AWB to take over and manage the irrigation and drainage system below
the distributaries/minors and subdrains feeding into branch drains operated by AWBs. Initially, the
existing FO pilots would be expanded gradually and modified to incorporate the lessons of experience or
research. The number would be expanded more rapidly on the basis of one or more successful models that
would emerge from the pilots. The legal framework for the institutional reforms has been established with
the enactment of the PIDA Acts in all Provinces. The Borrower also intends to implement other important
reforms. It intends to reorient the functions and organization of WAPDA's Water Wing towards
coordinated management and regulation of the Indus Basin; and  streamline W'APDA's organization,
internal policies and procedures to increase its overall efficiency. The Chairman of WAPDA outlined these
institutional reforms in a letter to IDA dated July 31, 1995.
1.26    Pilot Projects: Under LBOD, a pilot-oriented feasibility study for an AWB on Nara Canal
Command is being carried out, with funding from IDA's Institutional Development Fund (Grant No.
28879); and pilots of wholesale (distributary level) FOs are underway in Nawabshah, Sanghar, and



9
Mirpurkhas Districts with the International Irrigation Management Institute (IIMI) as the principal
consultant. Feasibility studies have recently been completed for AWBs on Chashma Right Bank Canal in
NWFP17 and Lower Bari Doab Canal in Punjab (Bank-Managed UNDP Grant). Under On-Farm Water
Management III (Ln/Cr. 2245/3327-PAK), three pilots of minor/distributary level FOs are underway (two
on minors in NWFP, and one on a distributary in Punjab). Under SCARP Transition II (Cr. 2257-PAK),
there has been overwhelmingly positive response from farmers, and ownership from Provinces, to the new
approach of using Social Organizers to help farmers establish FOs (around FGW tubewells transferred to
farmers), which was introduced after the Mid-term Review (MTR) in 1994. Since then, over 500 village-
level FOs have been established in Punjab and 70 in Sindh around FGW tubewells which are being
"transitioned" (transferred) from PIDs to fanners, and about 100 more are planned. Building on this
beginning, over 6,300 such village-level FOs are due to be established under the proposed Punjab Private
Sector Groundwater Development Project. Under the recently approved Balochistan Community Irrigation
and Agriculture Project (Cr. 2780-PAK), between 45-50 FOs will be established around community
irrigation facilities. The village-level FOs will later be consolidated at the minor/distributary levels under
future projects, to benefit from economies of scale.
E. EXTERNAL ASSISTANCE TO THE SECTOR
1.27   The Indus Basin Project: The Bank has a long history of collaboration and partnership with
Pakistan in irrigation and drainage development ever since Pakistan's independence in 1947. It remains
the principal donor to the sector. In 1960, the Bank helped negotiate the Indus Water Treaty between
Pakistan and India, with Pakistan acquiring rights to the Indus, Jhelum, and Chenab rivers. Shortly
thereafter, the Bank helped Pakistan establish the Indus Basin Development Fund, which financed the
Indus Basin Replacement Works Project (IBRWP). The IBRWP was completed to quality, under budget
and on schedule (within 10 years). IBRWP helped Pakistan build the world's largest contiguous surface
irrigation system. The IBRWP was instrumental in the creation and strengthening of the main institutions
currently dominating the water sector in Pakistan, notably WAPDA. In 1958, the Bank helped the
Borrower formulate the SCARPs program.
1.28   Planning Studies: In 1973, the Bank assisted Pakistan to formulate the Revised Action Plan,
which was the perspective plan for the sector's development in the 1970s. The physical targets of the
SCARPs and the Revised Action Plan programs have largely been achieved albeit behind schedule. In
1990, the Bank was again instrumental in the formulation of the Water Sector Investment Planning Study
(WSIPS), which served as a perspective plan for the Eighth Five Year Plan (1993-1998) that is currently
under implementation. The DSEA was the most recent sector-wide master planning study to be formulated
with the Bank's assistance. It will likely serve as the perspective plan for the Ninth Five Year Plan (1998-
2003).
1.29   External Assistance to the Sector: Since 1990, the World Bank, the Asian Development Bank
(ADB), Japan, and Germany have been the major sources of external assistance in the water sector in
Pakistan. Others who have provided assistance include Canada, France, the Netherlands, Sweden,
Switzerland, United Kingdom, European Union, the International Fund for Agricultural Development
(IFAD), and UNDP. The Bank has worked closely with the other external agencies in overcoming
many of the constraints affecting project implementation and operational performance identified earlier.
Since 1993, IDA has been collaborating very closely with other donors on the restructuring and supervision
turnaround of the $880 million LBOD (Cr. 1532-PAK); and actively assisting the Borrower in
strengthening public sector expenditure programming through the introduction of a three-year rolling
Public Sector Development Program (PSDP) for federally funded projects. This has already assisted in
accelerating the implementation of water sector projects by enhancing budget allocations and protecting
these projects against across-the-board budget cuts. The 1995/96 to 1997/98 PSDP provided a budget of
Rs 12.9 billion for irrigation and drainage projects for FY1995/96. Most external agencies see continued
17     For the proposed lift canal system, financed by Japan's International Cooperation Agency (JICA).



10
involvement in the water sector as important in protecting and enhancing the large investments that have
already been made, ensuring that their intended benefits are realized and improving the effective utilization
of the Indus river water resources.
1.30    The Lessons of Development Assistance Experience: The lessons of experience, reflected
frequently in reports published by IDA's OED on similar Bank/IDA-financed projects in Pakistan, Mexico,
Egypt, Indonesia, lndia, and Philippines, and from ongoing implementation of the LBOD Project (Cr.
1 532-PAK) are pertinent to the proposed project. They are: (i) irrigation development should be linked to a
proper drainage strategy and investment program; (ii) a long-term solution for disposal of drainage effluent
is essential for drainage; (iii) program or policy-based lending is preferred to project-based lending for
promoting reform and reducing the backlog/overhang of the portfolio of on-going projects; (iv)
Government/donor commitment to too many discrete projects can exacerbate the problem  of over-
commitment of resources; (v) a strong O&M program with reliable funding and cost recovery should be
instituted during implementation and sustained after completion; (vi) capabilities of key institutions to
implement projects in a timely fashion should be developed; (vii) farmer's involvement in design,
implementation, and O&M is essential for sustainability. The most effective way to achieve their
participation is to have beneficiaries bear and finance part of the construction costs, if necessary through
credit. Beneficiaries' assumption of part of the cost of the irrigation infrastructure, beginning at the outset
of the project, increases their commitment to use the infrastructure effectively, and to recover the money
invested in it; (viii) the only effective mechanism to increase cost recovery is to turn over parts of the
system to farmers; (jx) monitoring water use in irrigation projects is essential for the good management of
this scarce resource. Flow meters, a relatively low-cost item, should be provided as part of future projects;
(x) extension services should educate farmers about the importance of the meters and irrigation scheduling
to optimize crop production. In larger surface water schemes, measuring devices should be provided as a
matter of course at all distribution structures, including even the smallest farm outlets (as small as 50
hectares); (xi) beneficiaries organized in users' associations have demonstrated their responsiveness for
O&M of infrastructure transferred to them by Government; and (xii) lack of timely preparation of
feasibility studies and engineering designs has been a major constraint to the implementation of time-slice
projects. The design of the NDP project reflects the lessons of experience enumerated above.
1.31    Lessons from Research: The lessons from research on water rights in other countries are also of
interest"8: (i) there are very substantial welfare and income gains from permitting trade in water, at
whatever level; (ii) water rights should be separated from land; (iii) water rights should be allocated based
on historical usage, and surplus amounts to usage should be auctioned off; (iv) transaction costs for trading
in water are very low, and the availability of adjustable gates is helpful to facilitate such trading; (v)
environmental regulations need to be enforced; (vi) the Government has a role to play in resolving the
conflicts that could arise; and (vii) public subsidies for O&M of irrigation obstruct water market
development.
2. THE PROPOSED PROJECT
A. PROJECT OBJECTIVES, FRAMEWORK, AND STRATEGY
2.1     Project Objectives: The goal of the project is to implement the first phase of the Borrower's and
Provinces' 25-year NDP which is designed to minimize saline drainable surplus, and facilitate the eventual
evacuation of all saline drainable surplus from the Indus Basin to the Arabian Sea, and thereby restore
enviromnentally-sound irrigated agriculture in Pakistan. The objectives of the project are to improve the
efficiency of the irrigation and drainage system in Pakistan, and ensure its sustaeinability, through: (i)
establishing an appropriate policy environment and institutional framework, and strengthening capacity of
18     These results are summarized from Trading Water-Use Rights: What Can We Learn From Chile, by K.
William Easter and Robert Hearne, mimeo dated November 28, 1995.



11
sector institutions (i.e., carrying out the first phase of policy and institutional reforms in the water sector);
(ii) improving sector policies and planning; (iii) strengthening the technical foundations of and knowledge
base on irrigation and drainage; and (iv) improving the irrigation and drainage infrastructure network.
2.2    Project Framework: The project is the next logical step in a progressive evolution of Pakistan's
long-term effort to preserve and develop its most important physical resource -- namely the Indus River
Basin -- by strengthening the policy framework and institutional capacity for comprehensive management
of the Indus River Basin. The project does this by supporting and mainstreaming participatory approaches
to irrigation management which have been pilot-tested in Pakistan for over two decades; promoting
collaboration between key agencies of the Borrower and its Provinces inter alia through sharing of
information; strengthening real-time water management at basin and sub-basin levels; and  stimulating
national dialogue on the challenges and opportunities posed to the economy by the progressive
deterioration of its key institutions.
2.3    Institutional Appraisal and Sector Analysis:  The project has its origins in a multi-year
participatory process of analysis, identification, and preparation which was based on recommendations of
the Borrower's Water Sector Investment Planning Study (WSIPS, 1990); Eighth Five-Year Plan (1993-98);
and DSEA (1993); followed by IDA's sector strategy paper titled "Pakistan Irrigation and Drainage: Issues
and Options" (1994)'9. The DSEA, WSIPS, and IDA's sector report contain detailed functional and
strategic appraisals of Pakistan's key water sector institutions (i.e. WAPDA and PIDs), which are the
principal subjects of reform under NDP. "Steering Committees" with high-level representatives from the
Borrower, Provinces, WAPDA, and the PIDs directed and participated in the institutional appraisals
(including a series of seminars). In addition, a series of seminars and tours were organized by IDA's
Economic Development Institute (EDI) for opinion leaders in all concerned entities to countries which
have implemented (or are implementing) identical institutional reforms notably Turkey, Mexico, Chile,
United States, Argentina and Dominican Republic. Further appraisal or "assessments" of the institutions
was carried out during the preparation and appraisal of the project20. The appraisals confirmed that even
though the institutions retain substantial capacities to implement investment programs in the water sector,
they suffer from severe systemic deterioration of public sector agencies in Pakistan, especially in the
capacity and quality of services they provide. They all are in need of re-engineering to make them
adaptable to the sector's evolving requirements for basin-wide environmental management,
decentralization, and beneficiary participation. The two river basin master planning studies (WSIPS and
DSEA) demonstrated that a Sector Investment Project (SIP) or "program approach" with strong "river
basin orientation" would result in a shorter completion period and higher rates of return for investment
projects.
2.4    Reducing Saline Drainable Surplus: To reduce the drainable surplus, the project's strategy is to
improve water management through non-structural and structural interventions.   Non-structural
interventions would comprise: (i) institutional reforms to decentralize the irrigation and drainage system;
(ii) capacity building of key irrigation and drainage institutions (WAPDA , PIDAs, AWBs, and FOs); and
(iii) policies to improve the efficiency of water allocation. Structural interventions would include: (i)
modernization of canal system management; (ii) improvement of irrigation systems and watercourses (e.g.:
to reduce seepage); and (iii) ensuring proper O&M of canals and drains so that they function as designed.
2.5    Evacuating Saline Drainable Surplus: To evacuate the drainable surplus, the project's strategy
is to: (i) use environmentally sustainable methods for disposal of saline drainage effluent (including
diverting saline effluent away from freshwater lakes and rivers), and lay the groundwork for construction
of a permanent trunk drainage network connected to the sea via LBOD's Spinal Drain which would be
known as the NSDS; (ii) improve the planning and programming of irrigation and drainage projects inter
19     Report No. 11884-PAK: Pakistan Irrigation and Drainage: Issues and Options; The World Bank, March 25,
1994.
20     Government of Pakistan: Feasibility Study: National Drainage Program, May 1995.



12
alia to incorporate and entrench an RBM perspective in all aspects of water resources management by the
Borrower, Provinces, WAPDA, PIDAs, AWBs, and eventually FOs, and to provide the critical physical
linkages necessary to convert existing drainage systems into operating sub-basin systems; (iii) rehabilitate
and modernize drainage systems to remedy the effects of deferred O&M; and (iv) provide research
resources to Pakistan's main drainage research institutes to strengthen the technical foundations for
drainage, resolve important remaining technical issues in drainage, and develop locally-adapted design
criteria. Constructing drainage facilities is a necessary, but not sufficient, condition for achieving
sustainable salt balance in the IBIS. All drainage facilities fnanced under the project must also be linked
within sub-basins, and then to outfalls which must ultimately connect to the Arabian Sea either through the
LBOD Spinal Drain or via the Indus River. "RBM Orientation" will be an important theme in the technical
design of physical interventions, particularly those involving rehabilitation or expansion of surface
drainage infrastructure.
2.6    Project Design: The project has the following design features which distinguish it from previous
investment projects such as those listed above: (a) a sector-wide scope: it covers a wide range of relevant
policies, programs and projects within the country's irrigated agriculture sector; (b) a coherent sector
policy framework, notably the Borrower's WSIPS, DSEA, and Eighth Five-Year Plan, as well as IDA's
Country Assistance Strategy, Water Sector Policy Paper, and sector strategy paper entitled "Pakistan
Irrigation and Drainage: Issues and Options." The expenditure program for the project has been and will
be assessed according to the criteria outlined in these policy framework documents and Country Portfolio
Performance Reviews (CPPRs) through annual reviews of the PSDP which are conducted jointly by the
Borrower, Provinces, ADB, Japan's Overseas Economic Cooperation Fund (OECF), and IDA. The
Borrower embraced the policy framework outlined in these reports in its Letters of Sector Development
Policy to IDA21; (c) strong stakeholder control is embedded in the design, preparation, and selection criteria
for Investment Projects and during implementation of Investment Projects; (d) participation of the three
main donors for Pakistan's irrigated agriculture sector (IDA, ADB, and C)ECF); (e) a common
implementation arrangement for the investment program shared by the three main donors; and (f) a strong
anchor in the rolling PSDP for the water sector, as well as a close linkage to the amnual CPPR process.
2.7    Linkage with Future Investments: The Borrower and IDA anticipate that if NDP is successfully
implemented, it would be followed by the proposed National Irrigation Prograrn (NIP) which is under
preparation22, and by a series of NDPs and other associated investments in the water sector. For that
reason, the project includes funds to prepare a pipeline of major drainage projects to be implemented from
the second half of this project, and for the preparation of NDP II. On the other hand, if the pace of reforms
is slow due to lack of government commitment, implementation constraints, or resistance/lack of
commitment by WAPDA, PIDAs or AWBs for real change, the Borrower and IDA have agreed and
understand that the incremental financing commitments for irrigation and drainage investments under NDP
would be scaled back or canceled altogether after the MTR or 2-3 years; and this factor would be taken
into account in determining IDA's financing for additional investments in the water sector notably NIP and
the proposed Fourth On-Farm Water Management IV Project.
B. PROJECT SUMMARY
2.8    Summary Description: As stated earlier, the project is designed as a SIP. It consists of three
complementary components, with estimated total costs of $785 million equivalent, namely: (i) sector
planning and research component ($25.7 million) aimed at facilitating long-term sector and project
planning (notably for the NSDS to carry saline effluent from the drainage network to the sea) and
enhancing the technical foundations of drainage; (ii) the institutional reforms component ($57.7 million)
which is the most important component and is aimed at decentralizing PIDs, and streamlining and building
21     Dated August 21, 1995 and October 3, 1995.
22     The principal focus of the proposed NIP is to strengthen AWBs (four have been selected), modernize
irrigation infrastructure of PIDAs and AWBs, and strengthen FOs within the commands of selected AWBs.



13
the capacity of WAPDA, PIDAs, AWBs, On-farm Water Management Directorates of Provincial
Agricultural Department (PAD-OFWMs), and FOs; and (iii) the investment component ($683.1 million)
which is designed to improve the drainage and water management infrastructure in about 5 million hectares
and protect inland wetlands. The investments component would finance completion of ongoing projects,
rehabilitation and remodeling of completed drainage projects, and new projects which conform to or
support the overall strategies of the project. In addition, the project would finance the costs of coordination
and supervision by the Borrower, WAPDA, the Provinces, and NDP donors ($18.5 million). The project's
components are described in further detail below.
C. SECTOR PLANNING AND RESEARCH
(COST ESTIMATE: $25.7 MILLION)
2.9    Introduction: This component would consist of two sub-components: (i) sector planning and
policy studies which would provide capacity building and technical assistance for policy formulation and
sector and project planning; and (ii) research.
2.10    Sector Planning and Policy Studies (Cost Estimate: $10.4 million): This sub-component would
provide capacity building and technical assistance for: (i) policy-oriented studies on the sector to improve
sector policies; (ii) sector planning studies. The project would support various initiatives to strengthen
sector planning specifically the Annual Three-Year Rolling PSDP and the Country Portfolio Performance
Review CPPR in so far as they relate to the water sector. The Borrower already collaborates closely with
IDA, ADB, and OECF in the annual formulation of the PSDP and in the donors' CPPR; and (iii) feasibility
studies to prepare a pipeline of high priority projects for the sector to be financed under NDP or other
projects. This would include a feasibility study for the NSDS which is central to the long-term drainage
strategy and RBM program for the Indus Basin, including the legal framework for cost sharing and
operation among Provinces, the Borrower and WAPDA, the financing plan, and environmental and
resettlement aspects. Each sector planning and policy study would be commenced sufficiently early as to
ensure that it would be completed by December 31, 2003. WAPDA and Provinces would appoint
consultants to carry out the policy, sector planning and feasibility studies. Assurances were obtained at
Negotiations that WAPDA would engage sector planning and studies consultants with qualifications and
terms of reference satisfactory to IDA by June 30, 1998.
2.11    Pipeline of Sector Planning and Policy Studies: The pipeline of sector planning and policy
studies identified or for which proposals have been prepared includes: (i) Efficacy of Past Drainage
Investments; (ii) National Salinity Survey; (iii) Preparation of the Ninth Five Year Plan; (iv) National
Water Policy; (v) Water Rights; (vi) Gypsum Pricing, Marketing and Distribution; (vii) Promoting Private
Investment in Drainage; (viii) Exploitation and Regulation of FGW; (ix) Irrigation and Drainage Atlas of
Pakistan; (x) Long-Termn Financing Options for Irrigation and Drainage; (xi) Revenue Options and
Prospects for WAPDA's Water Wing; and (xii) Revenue Options for PIDAs, AWBs, and FOs. On the first
two studies, terms of reference have been finalized, and the procurement process has been initiated. These
studies are expected to commence in the first year of project implementation.
2.12   Research Programs (Cost Estimate: $15.3 million): The research sub-component would
support two broad initiatives: (i) Major Research Programs; and (ii) Small Grants Programs. The first
research initiative would support major research programs of the Borrower, through participating research
institutes (PRIs) and WAPDA. The second initiative would complement the major research programs by
providing the following types of grants to individuals and institutions (including PRIs): (a) Research
Preparation Grants to prepare proposals for major research programs, prioritize research programs, and
prepare research work programs; and (b) Research Dissemination Grants to prepare dissemination plans,
establish external collaboration arrangements, and/or disseminate research findings including workshops
and seminars to foster linkages between research institutes and sector-related agencies, farmers, and the
private sector. The sub-component would also help strengthen linkages between Pakistan and intemational
research institutions through training, seminars, twining arrangements, and other forms of collaboration.



14
2.13    Pipeline of Research Programs: A pipeline of potential major research programs has been
identified. Proposals for these programs have been received from research institutes. These include: (i)
Design and Management of Evaporation Ponds; (ii) Use of Brackish Drainage Water Effluent for
Agriculture and Forestry; (iii) Root Zone Salinity Management using Fractional Skimming Wells with
Pressurized Irrigation; (iv) Maintenance of Surface Drains; (v) Development of Design Criteria for Surface
Drainage System; (vi) Collaborative (Community) Pilot Tile Drainage; (vii) Institutional Framework for
Improved Sustainability and Productivity of Irrigated Agriculture in Pakistan; (viii) Impact of Pipe
Drainage Systems on Land and Water; (ix) Hydraulic and Water Quality Monitoring and Modeling of
Chenab, Jhelum and Indus Rivers; (x) Impact of Pipe Drainage Systems on Land and Water; (xi)
Networking System at Country and Global Level; and (xii) Protection of Drainage Water Quality from
Pollution by Municipal Effluent.
2.14    Implementation Plan: Procurement process for the following three major research programs
estimated to cost $3.3 million have been completed: (i) Design and Management of Evaporation Ponds; (ii)
Use of Brackish Drainage Water Effluent for Agriculture and Forestry; and (iii) Root Zone Salinity
Management using Fractional Skimming Wells with Pressurized Irrigation. Contracts for these research
programs are ready for signature, and are expected to be signed shortly after Board Approval.
Implementation would start shortly thereafter. The majority of the other identified research programs are
expected to commence in the second and third year of project implementation. Preparatory work on the
research contracts including all procurement actions would be carried out as to ensure that the research is
fully completed by December 31, 2003.
D. INSTITUTIONAL REFORMS
(COST ESTIMATE: $57.7 MILLION)
2.15    Introduction and Summary: This component would consist of two major sub-components:
(i) Institutional Reforms for WAPDA's Water Wing; and (ii) Institutional Reforms for PIDs. Both sub-
components, although not having the largest financial allocation, are by far thie most important sub-
components of the project. The objectives of the component are to improve the performance of WAPDA
and PIDs, by introducing institutional reforms. The essential elements of the institutional reforms are: (a)
redefining roles and functions; (b) decentralizing roles and responsibilities; (c) streamlining; (d)
transferring management responsibility for those functions which should be managed by other (lower tier)
entities which will succeed PIDs i.e. PIDAs, AWBs, and FOs, or by the private sector; and (e) capacity
building for WAPDA and the new public and private institutions. Training, gender and poverty focus, and
public participation are important themes in the capacity building and strategic recirientation programs for
all institutions to assist the Borrower and its Provinces to implement institutional reforms.
2.16    Critical Factors for Success: In order to increase the probability of the institutional reform
programs being successful and effective, key lessons from global experience in institutional change
management have and would be reflected in the design and implementation of the institutional reforms.
These include working with the Borrower and other stakeholders for: (a) establishing a shared sense of
urgency among the stakeholders; (b) articulating and envisioning the compelling short-, medium-, and
long-term vision for the institutions within the framework of evolving competitive environments; (c)
establishing effective guiding coalitions with key stakeholders, entities, and strategic partners inside and
outside Pakistan; (d) ensuring strong leadership by senior management and officials of the Borrower,
Provinces and WAPDA; (e) seeking, consolidating, and using extensively the programs of two-way and
multi-dimensional information, education, and communication with agency staff, the media and other
stakeholders; (f) rnaintaining full-time teams dedicated to the change effort at WAPDA and Provincial
levels; (g) using positive demonstrative effects of early successes; (h) engaging large numbers of staff in
the change effort; (i) embedding institutional change in the evolving cultures of the changed institutions; ()
intensively monitoring the change process including through staff and client surveys; (k) using outside
consulting assistance wherever necessary; and (1) maintaining and demonstrating a strategic, dynamic/



15
flexible, client-centered and pragmatic orientation towards the change process itself, with emphasis on the
process variables of change (as contrasted with a blue-print approach).
Institutional Reforms for WAPDA's Water Wing (Cost Estimate: $31.4 million)
2.17    Summary: The Chairman of WAPDA outlined the institutional reforms which the Senior
Management of WAPDA have envisaged for the Water Wing in a letter to IDA dated July 31, 1995. The
sub-component will help WAPDA to implement the program outlined in this letter. It would consist of
initiatives to assist WAPDA's Water Wing to implement its institutional program consisting of: (a)
strategic and RBM reorientation to focus on inter-Provincial (i.e. federal) roles, mandates, and functions;
(b) streamlining to improve capacity utilization, operational efficiency and effectiveness; (c) capacity
building, including public participation, financial management, and information systems; and (d) training.
The sub-component would provide consulting services to help WAPDA establish effective strategic
partnerships with similarly oriented institutions in Pakistan and elsewhere, training for staff and important
categories of clients such as contractors, field observation equipment, laboratory equipment, and computer
equipment and software, to support the initiatives outlined by WAPDA's Senior Management. WAPDA
would prepare, and agree with IDA on, a detailed Action Plan with a schedule for its Institutional Program;
and subsequently implement the Action Plan.
2.18   Strategic Reorientation & RBM: The strategic reorientation work program would support
reorientation of WAPDA's Water Wing to strengthen its role towards more strategically important and
federally oriented responsibilities, in particular those related to integrated development, management and
regulation of surface and ground water resources at the river basin level; monitoring of land and water
quality and environmental change; and planning, construction and O&M of inter-Provincial irrigation and
drainage infrastructure. WAPDA would shift its focus away from construction of infrastructure which can
be undertaken by Provincial agencies (i.e., intra-Provincial infrastructure) or which should be the
responsibility of the farmers or the private sector (notably on-farm drainage). The aim would be to reorient
the functions and organization of WAPDA's Water Wing towards coordinated management and regulation
of the Indus Basin (i.e. "RBM Orientation"). The role of WAPDA's Water Wing would gradually be
reoriented to inter-Provincial functions; and to undertake any work contracted to it by the Borrower,
Provinces, PIDAs, AWBs, and FOs. In addition, the project would assist WAPDA's Water Wing to enter
the Information Age in the 21st century by turning it from a construction agency to a "knowledge water
resources agency"; establishing more effective mutual dependencies, partnerships and collaborative
arrangements with Provincial entities (Environmental Protection Agencies - EPAs, PIDAs, PAD-OFWMs,
AWBs and FOs), and national, international, non-governmental, and private sector entities.
2.19   Streamlining: The streamlining work program would support efforts of WAPDA's Senior
Management to streamline its organization, intemal processes, and procedures to improve institution's
operating efficiency and its capacity to manage the Indus Basin as a unified hydrological entity. This
would involve a process of identifying and removing obstacles to the utilization of existing and potential
institutional capacity, and streamlining its operations and management.
2.20    Capacity Building: Capacity building is the largest initiative in the sub-component. It would
finance initiatives aimed at creating or strengthening institutional capabilities in WAPDA's Water Wing for
RBM through specific interventions in aspects of its operations such as environmental management, M&E,
public participation, financial management, information systems, procurement and contract administration,
planning, and succession planning. The component would also fund training programs which would be
formulated in consultation with OECF, ADB, and concerned agencies in Provinces. OECF has proposed
that UNDP (Pakistan) would manage, coordinate and supervise the training component financed by
OECF. Collaborative arrangements would also be established to ensure synergies between EDI's
Participatory Irrigation Management (PIM) Program and the priorities of WAPDA's Academies and
Colleges, notably on decentralization and irrigation management transfer. The expected retirement of a
substantial number of senior managers in WAPDA poses major challenges, but also presents unique
opportunities for renewal and reorientation of the institution. This project component would provide the



16
resources and framework to help the Borrower and WAPDA address the issue during the pivotal transition
phase for WAPDA.
Institutional Program for PIDs (Cost Estimate: $26.3 million)
2.21    Summary: This sub-component would include the costs of consultants (including financial and
management consultants, social organizers, service contracts with NGOs, and external auditors), vehicles,
computer equipment and software, staff training, alternative skills development and redundancy packages,
and incremental staff and operating costs to assist in the planning and implementation of the
decentralization of PIDs and management transfer of the irrigation and drainage infrastructure process. The
sub-component would support the following initiatives: (a) establishment of PIDAs, pilot AWBs and FOs
on pilot basis in each Province; (b) transitioning irrigation systems from the PIDAs to AWBs and FOs; and
(c) streamlining and cost reduction among the decentralized PIDAs, AWBs, and FOs to enable them to
carry out their functions; and (d) capacity building of the PIDAs, AWBs, FOs and PAD-OFWMs.
2.22    The Policy Framework:  In August 1995, the Borrower decided in consultation with its
Provinces and IDA and ADB that it would implement the strategy/vision agreed with IDA and ADB by
adopting institutional arrangements that were appropriate for Pakistan's socio-economic conditions. The
strategy and institutional reforms are outlined in the Borrower's Sector Development Policy Letters to
IDA. The reforms include a firm commitment and plan to phase out subsidies for O&M in seven to ten
years.
2.23    The Institutional Reforms: The Borrower and its Provinces intend to: (i) convert their PIDs into
autonomous PIDAs; (ii) transition fmancially self-accounting AWBs on a pilot basis from existing
"Circles" of PIDs in selected canal commands; and (iii) promote the formation of FOs on a pilot basis.
Pilot FOs owned and controlled by farmers would take over and manage the irrigation and drainage system
below the distributaries/minors, sub-drains feeding into branch drains operated by pilot AWBs, tubewells
and tile drains. While the Borrower, its Provinces and IDA agree on the direction of reform, the pace at
which the decentralization and management transfer process can or should be implemented is as yet
unclear. The likely trajectory of institutional reforms in each Province will depend on a number of factors
whose evolution is difficult to predict, but include: (i) first and most important, the commitment and
political will of the Borrower and its Provinces to exercise leadership in implementing the decentralization
and management transfer process; (ii) second, the implementation capacity of each Province and its
consultants to implement the decentralization process; (iii) third, the willingness and cooperation of the
affected farming population (notably the more influential feudal landlords); and (iv) fourth, the willingness
of PIDs (notably their staff) to embrace the reforms.
2.24   Legal Framework and Enabling Legislation: The Canal and Drainage Act of 1873 is the over-
riding legal framework for the irrigation and drainage sector. During project preparation, the NDP
Feasibility Study Consultants investigated the possibility of implementing the institutional reforms
envisaged under the project without enacting new legislation or amending existing legislation, and
concluded that at the minimum, amendments would be required. The PIDA Acts provide the legal
framework for establishment of PIDAs, AWBs, FOs, and regulation of the decentralized water sector. The
PIDA Ordinances were promulgated by all the Provinces in January 1997, in the absence of Provincial
Assemblies. Following the general election in February 1997, all the Provincial Assemblies have approved
the PIDA Acts, and all provinces have reenacted the PIDA Acts. Assurances were obtained at
Negotiations that the Provinces would establish at least one A WB in a canal command in each Province,
selected in consultation with IDA, within one year of the establishment of the PIDA.
2.25    Adequacy of the Enabling Legislation: Our review of the PIDA Acts has confirmed earlier
indications from dialogue with the Borrower and Provinces, that the Acts are adequate to implement the
institutional reforms envisaged under the project. However, there is still scope for improvement in the
following areas: (a) providing for more autonomy for PIDAs and AWBs; (b) clearly describing the roles
and responsibilities of AWBs, and in case of some Provinces of FOs; (c) describing the organization of the



17
AWBs; (d) providing for the establishment of a separate or quasi-autonomous agency to regulate and
adjudicate the decentralized irrigation and drainage institutions and infrastructure; (e) providing for fuller
and faster financial self-sustainability of PIDAs and AWBs (rather than partial self-sustainability - of
operation and maintenance costs only - over a 7-10 year time frame provided for in the Acts); and (t)
providing for faster financial self-sustainability of Farmer Organizations (rather than 7 years provided for
in the Acts). Nevertheless, the PIDA Acts are adequate in their present form. Subsequent refinements and
improvements are expected during implementation.  The above issues will be addressed during
implementation through dialogue, and as the political environment becomes more favorable. The
Borrower and the Provinces have agreed that they would annually review the PIDA Acts based on the
experience of implementation, and would agree with the NDP donors on any amendments and
improvements to the statute. This is reflected in the agreed Action Plan for the project (Annex 7).
2.26   Agreements and Safeguards: The strong commitment of the Borrower to implement the reforms
as indicated in the Sector Development Policy Letter and WAPDA's Institutional Program Letter,
promulgation of the PIDA Ordinances in all Provinces, their subsequent approval by Provincial
Assemblies, and reenactment by the Provinces have provided IDA with satisfactory basis for the financing
of the project. There however remains a risk that the Provinces may modify important provisions of the
PIDA Acts for whatever reason to the detriment of the project. Sufficient safeguards have been built into
the design of the project. The Borrower and IDA have agreed and understand that the incremental
financing commitments for irrigation and drainage investments under NDP would be scaled back or
canceled altogether after the MTR if the pace of reforms is slow due to lack of government commitment,
implementation constraints, or resistance/lack of commitment by WAPDA, PIDAs or AWBs for real
change.  This factor would be taken into account in determining annual incremental financing
commitments to participating agencies (PA) under the project, and in determining IDA's financing for
additional investments in the water sector. In addition, only PIDAs, AWBs and FOs which are established
and meet the eligibility criteria would be eligible to participate in the project23. The General Conditions
(Section 6), Development Credit Agreement (Section 5.01 e which specifies remedies of the Association in
the event of repeal or material modification of the PIDA Ordinance), and Project Agreement (Section 2.04
which specifies eligibility criteria for annual incremental fnancing commitments to PIDAs, AWBs and
FOs, and the Action Plan for the Project referred to in Section 2.01b) afford the necessary safeguards and
remedies to IDA.
2.27   Streamlining and Cost Reduction in Operations of Decentralized Irrigation Institutions: The
project would finance the costs of conducting more thorough and participatory institutional assessments;
redefining the mandates, and developing business plans; and introducing organizational adjustments,
structuring, and realigning levels' and composition of staff of PIDAs, pilot AWBs, and pilot FOs. To help
the new entities achieve significant cost reductions and improve their productivity, Provinces would
impose hiring freezes on all PIDAs and AWBs, reassign redundant staff to other organizations, and offer
them incentives for early retirement. The project would also provide assistance to PIDAs and AWBs to
"transition" (i.e. transfer) FGW tubewells, on-farm tile drains, and sub-drains to farmers through FOs. This
process is expected to generate significant cost savings. To this end, the project would finance the costs of
PIDAs, AWBs, and FOs to conduct "Diagnostic Reviews" and prepare Business or Operational Plans of
their irrigation and drainage operations.
E. INVESTMENTS
(COST ESTIMATE: $683.1 MILLION)
2.28   Introduction and Summary: This component would include construction and improvement of
drainage and irrigation facilities including: (a) rehabilitation, construction and improvement of off-farm
drainage; (b) on-farm drainage; (c) rehabilitation and modernization of irrigation systems; and (d)
operation and maintenance through performance-based contracts awarded to the private sector. The
23     Eligibility criteria for PAs are given in Annex 6.



18
component would finance a time slice of irrigation and drainage investments using a SIP approach
including: (i) civil works; (ii) equipment; (iii) cost of social organization, communication, information, and
education strategies, gender initiatives, and resettlement and rehabilitation associated with the construction
of drains; and (iv) consulting services to assist executing agencies to prepare Investment Projects, prepare
detailed designs and tender documents, evaluate bids and award contracts, and supervise construction.
This component would include: (i) completion of on-going projects; (ii) rehabilitation and remodeling of
completed projects; and (iii) new projects which meet the agreed selection criteria for Investment Projects.
2.29    Selection Criteria for Investment Projects: Assurances were obtained at Negotiations that
Investment Projects financed under the project would satisfy the Selection Criteria. Annex I describes the
selection criteria for Investment Projects, and the environmental evaluation procedures which will be
observed for all Investment Projects. The selection criteria, inter alia: (i) direct project interventions for the
investment component to those which would benefit the lowest third of the income strata of the farning
community; and (ii) provide for the project's investments to be implemented by Participating Agencies
(PAs) in areas under their legally recognized and clearly demarcated operational jurisdiction or for which
they are engaged (by the PA having operational jurisdiction) to implement an Investment Project under the
terms of a "Standard Construction Partnership and Hand-over Agreement", satisfactory to IDA.
Agreement was reached at Negotiations that the operating jurisdiction of the various PAs would be:
(a)    FO: (i) on-farm drainage; and (ii) on-farm irrigation upto minor/distributary level;
(b)    AWB  intra-provincial irrigation and drainage within its canal command (other than on-
farm drainage and irrigation);
(c)    PIDA: (i) intra-provincial irrigation and drainage outside canal commands; and (ii) intra-
provincial irrigation and drainage within canal commands in which AWBs have not been
egtablished (other than on-farm drainage and irrigation). This would be an interim
arrangement until AWBs have been established in all canal commands; and
(d)    WAPDA: inter-provincial irrigation and drainage.
2.30    Off-Farm Drainago (Cost Estimate: $426.6 million): The sub-component would include: (i)
rehabilitation, remodeling, improvement, and extension of existing surface drainage systems and
construction of new surface drains. This would include cross-drainage facilities (e.g., where roads, canals,
and railway lines block natural drainage); modification of existing systems and construction of new
interceptor systems for selected reaches of main canals; and facilities to keep storm water out of, and its
disposal from, drainage areas; (ii) rehabilitation of existing, and/or installation of new, SGW tubewells in
Punjab and Sindh, in areas unsuitable for tile drainage, and after ensuring that this is environmentally
acceptable and after the beneficiaries have organized themselves into FOs and have agreed to take over
responsibility for O&M.
2.31    On-Farm  Drainage (Cost Estimate: $92.6 million): This sub-component would include: (i)
construction of new, and rehabilitation of existing, sub-drains and on-farm surface drains and transfer of
these to beneficiaries; (ii) installation of new, and rehabilitation of existing, tile drains; (iii) improvement of
watercourses in SGW areas24; and (iv) transfer of existing FGW tubewells to, and where necessary, their
replacement by, the private sector, and expansion of the network of FGW tubewells through installation of
new tubewells by the private sector. Under all initiatives, beneficiary groups will be encouraged to adopt
biological drainage measures, where feasible, to reduce the drainable surplus. All on-farm investments
would only be undertaken where FOs have been established and are willing to share the investment costs
and to assume responsibility for a substantial proportion of O&M costs. Thus, the scope of this sub-
24     To ensure balance among the various initiatives in this component, improvement of watercourses would be
limited to a maximum of 2000 watercourses.



19
component will depend critically on the ability of the concerned PIDAs/AWBs to promote the
establishment of effective FOs; and the willingness of those FOs to undertake the investments on the
proposed terms. The sub-component will be accompanied by programs of education, communication,
gender focus, and social mobilization which will be designed and executed in close collaboration with
NGOs and community organizations.
2.32    Rehabilitation and Modernization of Irrigation Systems (Cost Estimate: $91.8 million): This
sub-component would include: (a) modernizing canal system management through installation of improved
communication and control systems and training of personnel; and (b) rehabilitation, improvement and
remodeling of irrigation systems, where the management of the system is constrained by the systems'
physical condition, after actual review of the irrigation supplies and demand and water allocation and
management practices. This is a sequel to the Second Irrigation System Rehabilitation Project (ISRP II,
Cr. 1888-PAK), which was closed on December 31, 1995; and has been included at the specific request of
the Borrower's highest authorities. In conformity with the project's objective of reducing the drainable
surplus in SGW areas, the rehabilitation and improvement of irrigation systems would focus on SGW areas
where FOs have been established.
2.33    O&M through Performance Contracts (Cost Estimate: $72.1 million): This sub-component
would support the carrying out of O&M of selected drainage facilities and maintenance of selected
irrigation facilities through multi-year (three to five years) contracts awarded to the private sector. This
initiative is intended to promote the role of the private sector in drainage and irrigation O&M, and thereby:
(a) ensure that adequate O&M of irrigation and drainage facilities is carried out under the framework of
fully-funded performance-based contracts, and thereby that the facilities function as designed; and (b)
reduce the cost of O&M through efficiency gains from competition and private sector involvement25. The
initial experience from the O&M Performance Contracts, awarded by WAPDA on a pilot basis for the
LBOD drainage facilities, was extremely positive26. The Performance Contracts which would be fimded
under the project would substantially be for O&M of projects/schemes which have been recently
completed or which would be completed shortly, and of newly constructed or rehabilitated facilities under
the project2". The O&M costs which would be funded under the project would therefore not substitute
existing funding of O&M by the Government or beneficiaries. The O&M costs which would funded under
the project would constitute only a small portion (about 7%) of the total O&M costs of the irrigation and
drainage system2". Recovery of all O&M costs29 would be carried out under the framework agreed for
eligibility of PAs to participate in the project. Under this framework, PIDAs and AWBs shall be
financially self-sustainable for O&M costs within ten years of their establishment; and FOs shall be
financially self-sustainable within seven years of their establishment.
2.34    Pipeline of Investment Projects:  A pipeline of potential Investment Projects has been
identified30. These Investment Projects would be included subject to their conforming with the agreed

2.35   Implementation Plan: Procurement for activities which would be carried out in the first year of
project implementation have been substantially completed. Contracts with estimated cost of US$66.1
million equivalent to be fnanced from the IDA Credit have already been signed. These include contracts
with the NDP Engineering Design and Supervision Consultants (US$42 million) and works on the LBOD
Project ($24.1 million). In addition, bid evaluations for contracts with estimated cost of US$9.8 million
equivalent to be financed from the IDA Credit have been completed. These contracts are expected to be
signed before or shortly after Board approval. During the first two years of implementation, the focus
would be on preparation of Investment Projects which conform to the selection criteria. A major
prerequisite for this viz., the engagement of the NDP Engineering, Design and Supervision Consultants
(who would assist in preparation and implementation of Investment Projects) has been completed; and this
is expected to provide substantial momentum to the preparation of Investment Projects. The Investment
Projects which would be commenced in the first two years of project implementation would substantially
comprise completion of the on-going LBOD project (estimated cost: US$166 million), O&M of recently
completed projects (notably LBOD - estimated cost US$46 million), and small surface drainage schemes
with short preparation period. Preparation, appraisal, design, and procurement actions for Investment
projects would be carried out as to ensure that the Investment Project is fully completed before the
projected completion date of the Project. Assurances were obtained at Negotiations that WAPDA and
Provinces would complete all detailed designs and award contracts for Investment Projects so as to ensure
that all such contracts, including the maintenance period specified in such contracts, will be completed not
later than December 31, 2003.
3. PROJECT COSTS AND FINANCING
A. PROJECT COSTS AND FINANCING PLAN
3.1    Project Costs: Project costs are tentatively estimated at $785 million, comprising $712.9 million
of investment costs and $72.1 million of recurrent costs. Recurrent costs comprise mainly of costs of
O&M through Performance Contracts awarded to the private sector ($67.7 million). Project costs include
$103.3 million of taxes and duties.  Base costs are estimated at about $729 million; while price
contingencies are estimated at $56 million. About $218.3 million of costs (28 percent of total costs) are
expected to be foreign exchange. The costs are only indicative, would be revised yearly during the
Borrower's annual budget cycle, and confirmed only after the MTR, by which time the scope of the project
in general, and investment component in particular, would have become more definitive. Project costs are
summarized in the Table below. More details are given in Annex 2.

3.3     IDA Financing: IDA would finance 42 percent of the project costs net of taxes, or 36 percent of
total costs including taxes with a Credit of SDR 198.6 million (equivalent of $285 million). IDA would
finance 40 percent ($87 million) of the direct and indirect foreign exchange costs, and 35 percent ($198
million) of local costs. IDA would finance about 32% of the Investment component ($219.1 million
equivalent); about 100% of the Sector Planning and Research component ($25.7 million equivalent); about
48% of the Institutional Reform component ($27.5 million equivalent); and about 69% of the program
coordination and supervision costs ($12.7 million equivalent). IDA would approve incremental financing
commitments during the annual NDP PSDP reviews, which would be held in Spring of each year. After
the project's MTR in Spring 2000, IDA may cancel a portion of the undisbursed commitments if it
32      Includes taxes and duties ($103.3 million).



22
concludes that progress is insufficient to justify continuation of the level of undisbursed financing
commitments or if there is insufficient movement on the institutional reforms.
3.4    Retroactive Financing: Eligible expenditures incurred after April 30, l99633 upto a limit of 10
percent of the Credit amount i.e., SDR 19.85 million ($28.5 million equivalent) would be eligible for
retroactive financing. These would include funds for start-up activities which are essential for timely
project implementation (such as feasibility studies, and detailed designs and engineering), and civil works
on Investment Projects for which construction has begun before Effectiveness, provided that procurement
procedures acceptable to IDA were followed, and in case of Investment Projects that the projects meet the
selection criteria. This is justified since the project is financing a time-slice of eligible Investment Projects
included in the Government's program; and one of the project components includes completion of on-
going projects.
3.5    Cofinancing Arrangements: The ADB has approved a loan of $140 million equivalent (SDR
93.674 million) from its Asian Development Fund (ADF) in December 1995; while Japan's OECF has
approved a loan of about $100 million equivalent (Yen 10,832 million)34. Both these donors would
participate in the project on the basis of parallel financing. Fulfillment of Conditions of Effectiveness of the
ADB Loan and the OECF Loan are Conditions of Effectiveness. By informal understanding reached among
cofinanciers, ADB would finance most Investment Project costs and program coordination and supervision
costs in Balochistan and NWFP, the costs of remaining contracts from its components under the ongoing
LBOD Project, some components of the RBOD Project, and some technical assistance costs for
environmental management and monitoring and evaluation. OECF would finance most Investment Project
costs in Punjab Province, some components of the RBOD Project; and most training costs. IDA would
finance the rest of the donors' share of project costs. This would include all Investment Project costs in
Sindh Province, costs of remaining works under the ongoing LBOD Project, some components of the
RBOD Project, and all remaining technical assistance and program coordination and supervision costs.
IDA would retain the option to reduce the undisbursed balance from its Credit by canceling an amount
equivalent to any additional cofnancing mobilized, after taking into account the revised cost estimates and
financing requirements. IDA, ADB, and OECF would enter into an agreement for cooperation,
coordination and exchange of information, which would inter alia define the roles and responsibilities of
the cofinanciers.
3.6    Counterpart Financing from the Borrower and its Provinces: The financial contributions of
the Borrower and Provinces for the project is estimated at $227.9 million equivalent (29 percent of project
costs) including for duties and taxes. The contributions from the Borrower and Provinces are projected to
increase from $5.6 million equivalent in FY97/98 (1st year) to a peak requirement of $42.5 million
equivalent in FY01/02 (5th year). The average annual contributions from the Borrower and Provinces
would amount to approximately $32.6 million equivalent over the seven years of disbursements.
3.7    Contributions from Beneficiaries: The contribution from beneficiaries' (mainly through FOs) is
estimated at $32.1 million (4 percent of project costs). Beneficiaries, through FOs, would contribute
between 10-40 percent of direct cash costs of on-farm Investment Projects upfront, in addition to
contributions of land and labor. This would replace in most cases the current practice of back-end cost
recovery which has been shown to have high administrative costs, and is prone to defaults and frequent
politically motivated rescheduling and/or waivers of payment obligations of farme:rs to Provinces.
33     The cut-off date for retroactive financing was agreed at Negotiations, and was based on the estimate at

34     Loan Agreements with ADB and OECF were signed on December 6, 1996 and March 31, 1997,

B. LENDING TERMS AND CONDITIONS
3.8    Terms of the IDA Credit: The IDA Credit to the Borrower would be on standard IDA terms with
a maturity of 35 years. With an implementation period of six and half years, the project would be
completed by June 30, 2004 and the Closing Date for the Credit would be December 31, 2004.
3.9    On-lending Terms and Provincial Allocations: The Borrower would retain part of the funds
from the Credit for the purposes of its activities under the project; and also would make funds available to
WAPDA and the Provinces on a first-come-first-served basis, in accordance with its standard budgetary
procedures for development expenditures. Available funds from the project would not be allocated to
WAPDA or Provinces on the basis of pre-determined shares, but on merit based on readiness of the
pipeline of Investment Projects and the demonstrated absorptive capacity of WAPDA or Provinces.
3.10   Provision of Funds by Provinces to PIDAs, AWBs and FOs: Provinces would make available
IDA funds, together with their own counterpart contributions, to eligible PIDAs, AWBs, and FOs, on a
first-come-first-served basis taking into account the demonstrated absorptive capacity of each entity, as a
grant and on the terms stipulated in a Participation Agreement between the Provinces and the entity.
Agreement was reached at Negotiations that Provinces would enter into Participation Agreements,
satisfactory to IDA, with each PIDA, A WB and FO proposing to participate in the Project. Aspects which
would be covered in Participation Agreements are given in Annex 3.
3.11   Incremental Financing Commitments: IDA would make incremental fmancing commitments
annually for new programs of activities to be commenced in each Fiscal Year based on the "Consolidated
NDP Three-Year Rolling Business Plan" (the Business Plan). The Borrower would submit the draft
Business Plan for the project to IDA by March 31 of each year, and agree the Plan with IDA by April 30 of
each year. The Plan would include procurement, staffing, operations, and fmnancing plans and encompass
all components and PAs of the project. Agreement was reached at Negotiations that the Borrower would,
not later than April 30 of each Fiscal Year ofproject implementation, or such later date as IDA may agree,
beginning Fiscal Year 96-97, furnish to IDA for its approval, proposals for amounts to be allocated out of
the proceeds of the Credit for financing any new program of activities to be commenced under the project
in the next following Fiscal Year of project implementation. IDA would not approve any proposals or
awards of contracts for Investment Projectsfurnished to it unless and until:
(a)    the Borrower has, with the participation of WAPDA and the Provinces, prepared and
furnished to IDA:
(i)    a consolidated Annual Progress Report (APR) on the carrying out of the
programs of activities previously commenced under the Project;
(ii)    a Three-Year Rolling PSDP for the project for the next three following Fiscal
Years ofproject implementation;
(iii)    a Portfolio Action Plan for the project for the next following Fiscal Year of
project implementation; and
(iv)    a Three-Year Rolling Business Plan for the project, which would also include
procurement and disbursement targets, for the next three following Fiscal Years
ofproject implementation; and
(b)    IDA is satisfied with the progress made in the carrying out of:
(i)    the programs of activities previously commenced under the Project; and
(ii)   the current Portfolio Action Plan and Three-Year Rolling Business Plan for the
Project.



24
C. PROCUREMENT
3.12    Procurement Assessment and Management: A formal Country Procurement Assessment (CPA)
was completed for Pakistan, and the Country Procurement Assessment Report (CPAR) is expected to be
published soon. Work that is nearly complete will result in country-specific Standard Bidding Documents
(SBDs) which are agreed between IDA and the Borrower. WAPDA, the key implementing agency for
NDP, has been the central partner with IDA in this review process, and (together with the Pakistan
Engineering Council, PEC) is helping IDA develop SBDs for Pakistan. Notvithstanding the recently
observed deterioration of institutional capacities of WAPDA and PIDs in procurement and contract
administration, the Borrower, Provinces, and WAPDA in particular, have extensive and generally
satisfactory records of compliance with IDA procurement procedures. Two comprehensive reviews of
WAPDA's procurement and contract administration procedures were undertaken by consultants appointed
by WAPDA following the recommendations of the MTR and 1993 Joint Review Mission for LBOD, and
as part of the Feasibility Study for NDP in 1994. The reviews identified areas of weakness (mostly having
to do with delays in decision making) and made recommendations for improvement which are incorporated
as part of the Institutional Program for WAPDA, specifically engagement of consultant procurement
specialists to overcome institutional shortcomings, and mandatory assignment of NDP Design and
Engineering Consultants as the Engineer on all civil works contracts fmanced under the project.
3.13    The Borrower's Procurement Arrangements: Taking these factors into account, the Appraisal
Mission concluded that the Borrower, WAPDA, prospective PIDAs, AWBs, and FOs would be capable of
handling procurement, supervising contract administration, and engaging and supervising consultants
envisaged under NDP with the help of procurement consultants and establishment of an NDP Procurement
Unit. WAPDA would also appoint one or more consultants with internationally recognized professional
qualifications in procurement administration, from NDP Engineering Design and Supervision Consultants
or other consultants, as NDP Procurement Advisers to provide technical assistance and training to staff of
WAPDA, PIDAs, AWBs, and FOs on procurement.
3.14   Procurement Elements and Plan: Under the proposed project, IDA will finance civil works for
construction (including rehabilitation and improvement of drains, canals, watercourses, and tubewells, and
performance-based contracts for O&M of irrigation and drainage infrastructure); equipment and vehicles;
and consultant services (including Research Contracts). WAPDA, PIDAs, AWBs and FOs will prepare
detailed Three-Year Rolling Procurement Plans as part of their Three-Year Rolling Business Plans. The
NDP Coordination Cell in WAPDA will consolidate the procurement plans of PAs, and submit the
combined Three-Year Rolling Procurement Plan for IDA's review and approval by March 31, together
with the project's Combined Business Plan. Contracts for civil works will be considered only as part of
complete Investment Projects and be awarded only after the Investment Project has been approved by IDA.
IDA's Guidelines for Procurement under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits would be ifollowed for procurement
of all works and goods financed from the Credit. Consultants financed from the Credit would be selected
following IDA's Guidelines for Selection and Use of Consultants. Procurement arrangements are detailed
in Annex 4, and summarized in the following paragraphs.

3.15    Works - Procurement Method:  All IDA-financed contracts for works estimated to cost the
equivalent of $5 million or more would be procured using International Competitive Bidding (ICB)
procedures (estimated cost: $123.1 million). IDA-financed contracts for civil works estimated to cost less
than the equivalent of $5 million each would be awarded using: (a) National Competitive Bidding (NCB)
procedures (estimated cost: $66.6 million); and (b) direct contracting, using project beneficiary labor
mobilized by PAD-OFWMs and FOs, with progress payments based on unit prices agreed with IDA
(estimated cost: $17.3 million). Civil works contracts may be bid on a slice-and-package basis, with the
contractors bidding for individual or combined contracts up to their pre-qualified limit.
3.16    Works - Prior Review: The following IDA-financed contracts would be subject to IDA's prior
review: (a) contracts for civil works estimated to cost more than $5 million equivalent; (b) the first NCB
contract by each PA; (c) O&M Performance Contracts, turnkey or supply-and-erect contracts (e.g. for
dredging equipment); and (d) contracts for borderline services such as GIS, aerial and satellite mapping.
All IDA-financed contracts not subject to IDA's prior review would be subject to post review on a sample
basis.
3.17    Works - Procurement Documents: Standard Bidding Documents (SBDs) for Pakistan would be
used after these are finalized by the Borrower, WAPDA and IDA. Until these are fnalized, IDA's SBDs
for Procurement of Works would be used for ICB contracts; and IDA's SBDs for Procurement of Works
(Smaller Contracts) with modifications agreed with IDA would be used for NCB contracts. Pre-
qualification would be undertaken for all contracts (or groups of contracts awarded on slice-and-package
basis) estimated to cost $15 million or more. Pre-qualification would be updated annually. Pre-

qualification would be done using IDA's Standard Pre-qualification Document for Works. All pre-
