3.18    Goods: All IDA-financed contracts for goods estimated to cost $300,000 equivalent or more
would be procured using ICB procedures (estimated cost: $15.9 million). The remaining IDA-financed
contracts for goods would be procured using NCB procedures (estimated cost: $2.4 million), except those
contracts costing less than the equivalent of $50,000 which will be procured following National Shopping
procedures (estimnated cost: $0.4 million) by obtaining quotations from at least three reputable suppliers.
The following IDA-financed contracts would be subject to IDA's prior review: (a) contracts for goods and
equipment estimated to cost $300,000 or more; (b) the first NCB contract by each PA; and (c) contracts for
borderline services such as GIS, aerial and satellite mapping. All IDA-financed contracts not subject to
IDA's prior review would be subject to post review on a sample basis. SBDs for Pakistan would be used
after these are finalized by the Borrower, WAPDA and IDA. Until these are finalized, IDA's SBDs for
Procurement of Goods would be used for contracts awarded using ICB procedures.
3.19   Documents To be Reviewed Under Prior Review: In case of contracts for goods and works
subject to prior review, each of the following stages and related documentation in the procurement process
would be reviewed and commented upon by IDA before they are implemented by the Borrower or PA: (a)
advertising procedures; (b) pre-qualification invitation documents, and subsequent evaluation; (c) bidding
documents and addenda; (d) bid evaluation and the proposal for contract award; (e) contract documents, to
identify any differences from the proforma contract included in the bidding documents and to verify
consistency with bid evaluation; and (f) material modifications to the contract during execution. Since the
project involves numerous procurements of similar goods, works, and services by all PAs, most
commercial documents would be common to similar items of procurement, and hence would be reviewed
once and then used repetitively. Only modifications to the standard document to suit specific procurement,
if any, would be submitted to IDA each time for prior review.
3.20   Consultancy Services: All consultants whose services are financed by IDA will be selected by
PAs in accordance with procedures outlined in IDA's "Guidelines for Use of Consultants by World Bank
Borrowers and by the World Bank as Executing Agency (August 1981)". [DA's Standard Letter of
Invitation and Standard Form of Contract for Consultants' Services will be used for consultancy services.
The following IDA-financed contracts would be subject to IDA's prior review: (a) consultancy contracts
with firms with estimated cost of $100,000 or more, and with individuals with estimated cost of $50,000 or
more; (b) the first contract for appointment of consultants for each PA; (c) contracts with finns awarded on
single-source basis; and (d) consultancy contracts for assignments of a critical nature (e.g., because of
environmental, safety, or public health considerations, Panels of Experts, Consultants for the Enhanced
Supervision Arrangements, Sector Planning and Research, Monitoring and Evaluation including
Environmental Monitoring and Audit, Social Assessments, Resettlement, Environmental Assessments,
Information Systems, and Financial Management/Accounting and Auditing), regardless of value. Terms of
reference for all IDA-financed consultancy contracts would be subject to prior review, regardless of value.
In case of consultancy contracts which are subject to IDA's prior review, the Borrower would submit for
IDA's review relevant documents at each stage of the process by which the Borrower selects consulting
firms including: (a) preparation of terms of reference and budget; (b) choice of selection procedure; (c)
preparation of the shortlist; (d) Letter of Invitation; (e) submission of the winning proposal or the
evaluation report; and (f) negotiation of the draft contract. All IDA-financed contracts not subject to prior
review would be subject to post review on sample basis.
3.21   In addition to the above provisions, the following would be reviewed by IDA (for goods, works,
and consultancy contracts): (i) any case in which the procedures used are not transparent or award
recommendation is irregular; (ii) any case in which a complaint has been raised; (iii) any request for re-
bidding; (iv) standard procurement documents to be used under the project; (v) proposals for extension of
bid validity, or delays in contract award; (vi) all advance procurement action, advance contracting, and
procurement relating to retroactive financing for ICB and NCB contracts; (viii) all contracts which are



27
proposed to be awarded to entities, companies, or agencies which are part of, owned or controlled by, the
Borrower, its Provinces, WAPDA, or any other PA; and (viii) sole-source procurement contracts.
3.22    Since the pipeline of Investment Projects is as yet not known with any degree of firmness, the
number of contracts which are likely to fall under the prior review threshold cannot be determined at this
stage, but is estimated to be about $123.1 million for works (60% of IDA-financed contracts), $15.9
million for goods (85% of IDA-financed contracts), $76.9 million for consultancy services (98% of IDA-
financed contracts), and $14.2 million for research contracts (100% of IDA-financed contracts). About
67% of all IDA-financed contracts would be covered by prior review, which is below the desirable level of
80%, but is acceptable because it has been selected to strike a balance between the work load for IDA on
prior review and an acceptable coverage for IDA-financed contracts in number and value, given the
potentially large number of PAs (several Regions and Divisions of WAPDA, 4 PIDAs, 4 PAD-OFWMs, at
least 4 AWBs, and many more FOs) and the likelihood that the project will consist of a large number of
small contracts or packages (especially for Investment Projects sponsored by FOs). Moreover, it is
justified by the following circumstances: (i) the Borrower, WAPDA, and Provinces have demonstrated
experience and capacity in procurement matters on similar Bank/IDA-financed projects; (ii) technical
assistance would be provided under the project to support their organizations and conduct prior review of
procurement decisions of PAs; (iii) each agency would execute a large number of similar repetitive
contracts; (iv) IDA and other NDP donors would conduct more intensive post reviews of procurement
decisions based on documentation prepared by the project's supervisory consultants; and (v) the
Borrower's ability, and indicated willingness to accept responsibility for the risk of misprocurement,
resulting in cancellation of Credit proceeds.
3.23    Post Review: The prior review will be complemented by post review of a random sample of
awarded contracts and procurement-related documentation below the prior review thresholds; in the
following proportions: (i) for works, 1 in 20 of an estimated 883 such contracts; (ii) for goods, 1 in 15 of
an estimated 125 such contracts; and (iii) for consultancy services, 1 in 5 of an estimated 100 such
contracts. The post review would cover the final contract and any evaluation of proposals and award
recommendations. In case misprocurement is detected during post review, IDA will immediately institute
a more intensive review of past and future contract documentation for procurement undertaken by the
concerned PA. Contracts financed under parallel financing arrangements (e.g. by OECF and ADB) would
also be subject to post review by IDA on a sample basis to ensure their compatibility with the balance of
the project, satisfactory quality, timely delivery or completion, and acceptable pricing.
D. DISBURSEMENTS
3.24   Summary: The Borrower's Ministry of Finance will allocate funds from the IDA Credit to the
Borrower's Ministry of Water and Power (MW&P), its Planning Commission, Provinces and WAPDA as
part of their Annual Development Plan (ADP) funds. The Project would be completed on June 30, 2004
and the Closing Date of the Credit would be December 31, 2004. The estimated'disbursement period is
seven years, which is only about one year shorter than the latest subsector disbursement profile for
irrigation and drainage projects in Pakistan. The shorter disbursement period is projected on account of the
demonstrated experience of program-type (time-slice) operations disbursing faster than traditional types of
project operations in Pakistan (by 2-3 years). Disbursement arrangements including use of Statements of
Expenditures and Special Accounts are detailed in Annex 5.
3.25    Special Accounts:  To facilitate disbursements, Special Accounts in US Dollars would be
established in the National Bank of Pakistan, as follows:
(a)    MW&P: the Secretary, MW&P, Islamabad, with an initial deposit of up to $100,000, to
finance the costs of Federal Coordination and Supervision. The initial deposit would be
$50,000 until the aggregate deposits into this Special Account equal $200,000;



28

(i)    the General Manager (Finance) in Lahore, with an initial deposit of up to $2
million, to finance the costs of WAPDA Coordination, Institutional Reforms for
WAPDA's Water Wing and the Sector Planning and Research Component. The
initial deposit would be $1 million until the aggregate deposits into this Special
Account equal $4 million;
(ii)   the General Manager (Water-South), in Hyderabad, with an initial deposit of up
to $5 million, to finance the costs of the Investment Component in Sindh
executed by WAPDA. The initial deposit would be $2.5 million until the
aggregate deposits into this Special Account equal $10 million;
(c)    Province of Sindh: the NDP Provincial Coordinator, Sindh Province, in Karachi or

Coordination, Institutional Reforms for PIDs, and Investment Component in Sindh
executed by the Province of Sindh. The initial deposit would be $2.5 million until the

(d)    Province of Punjab: the NDP Provincial Coordinator, Punjab Province, in Lahore, with

and Institutional Reforms for PIDs in Balochistan Province executed by the Province of
Balochistan. The initial deposit would be $0.25 million until the aggregate deposits into
this Special Account equal $1 million.
3.26   These deposits are based on estimates of the project's financing needs and projected
disbursements from the Special Accounts, including retroactive financing. W/As for replenishment of
Special Accounts would be submitted on a monthly basis, or when 20% of the authorized limit of the
Special Account has been utilized, whichever occurs earlier.
3.27    Statements of Expenditures (SOEs): IDA would disburse against Certified SOEs for the
following types of expenditures: (i) on contracts for works of less than $5,000,000 equivalent; (ii) on
contracts for equipments and vehicles of less than $300,000 equivalent; (iii) on contracts for consultants
services of less than $100,000 in the case of firms, and less than $50,000 in the case of individuals; (iv) for
training expenditure; and (v) for incremental staff and operating costs. All other expenditures would be
supported by full documentation.
3.28   Allocation of Credit Proceeds: All amounts of IDA financing would remain unallocated between

Province. IDA would not finance any costs relating to duties and taxes. Amotnts would be reallocated

commitments for that year (see para 3.11 above). The allocation among categories (given in the table

4. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
A. THE PARTICIPATING AGENCIES (PAS)
4.1    Responsibilities of PAs: The Borrower would have overall responsibility for coordinating and
monitoring project implementation. The Sector Planning sub-component would be implemented by the
Borrower, WAPDA's Water Wing and Provinces depending on the nature of the studies. The Drainage
Research sub-component would be implemented by Research Institutes, under a contract with WAPDA's
Water Wing. The Institutional Reforms for WAPDA's Water Wing would be implemented by WAPDA's
Water Wing; while that for PIDs would be implemented by Provinces through PIDAs, AWBs, PAD-
OFWMs, and FOs. Investment Projects would be carried out by the various PAs which have operational
jurisdiction over the Investment Project. FOs would carry out on-farm drainage, and on-farm irrigation
upto minor/distributary level, both with assistance from PAD-OFWMs. AWBs would carry out intra-
provincial irrigation and drainage (other than on-farm drainage and irrigation) within their respective canal
commands. PIDAs would carry out intra-provincial irrigation and drainage (ot]her than on-farm drainage
and irrigation) outside canal commands and within canal commands in which AWBs have not been
established. WAPDA's Water Wing would carry out inter-provincial irrigation and drainage. WAPDA's
Environmental Cell and Provincial EPAs would have the lead role in preparation, review, approval, and
implementation of environmental aspects of the project including Environmental Impact Assessments
(EIA), Environmental Management Plans (EMP), environmental audits, and Information, Education, and
Communication (IEC) campaigns for the activities to be carried out by WAPDA and Provinces
respectively.
B. ELIGIBILITY CRITERIA AND APPROVAL OF PROGRAMS
4.2    Eligibility Criteria for PAs: The PAs are indicated in para 4.1 above. Additional PAs may be
added on terms and conditions satisfactory to IDA. Assurances were obtained at Negotiations that each
PA would satisfy the specified eligibility criteria to be eligible for incremental annual financing
commitments from the IDA Credit for new programs of activities to be commenced under the Project, and
any new contracts to be awarded by it for on-going Investment Projects. The agreed eligibility criteria are
detailed in Annex 6. Eligibility criteria for PIDAs, AWBs and FOs inter alia include that they: (i) are
established and operated as autonomous entities; (ii) have prepared and agreed with the NDP Coordination
Cell a Three Year Rolling Business Plan including demonstrating progress towards financial self-
sustainability of O&M costs for PIDAs and AWBs within ten years of establishment, and financial self-
sustainability for FOs within seven years of establishment; and (iii) have achieved satisfactory progress in
implementation of their Business Plan for the previous Fiscal Year including achieving targets for cost
reduction, O&M expenditure and collecting Abiana and cost-sharing (beneficiary contribution) amounts.
Eligibility criteria for PAD-OFWMs inter alia include that they have achieved satisfactory progress in
implementation of their Business Plan for the previous Fiscal Year including collecting cost-sharing
(beneficiary contribution) amounts. Eligibility criteria for WAPDA inter alia include that it has achieved



31
satisfactory progress in implementation of its institutional reform program and its Business Plan for the
previous Fiscal Year.
4.3    Approval of Programs and Investment Projects: Agreement was reached at Negotiations that
institutional reform programs, research and sector planning programs, Investment Projects, and program
coordination and supervision activities financed from the IDA Credit would be approved by IDA. As
indicated earlier, Investment projects financed under the project would satisfy the Selection Criteria for
Investment Projects.
C. PROJECT COORDINATION AND SUPERVISION
4.4    Action Plan: Annex 7 presents the agreed action plan for the project, which provides a summary
of the main actions which the Borrower and PAs would take in order to implement the project on schedule.
4.5    Overall Program Coordination & Monitoring: Overall program coordination and monitoring
would be the responsibility of the Federal Project Steering Committee (FPSC), which would be chaired by
the Secretary of the MW&P of the Borrower. Members of the FPSC include the Federal Secretaries for
Finance, Planning and Development, Economic Affairs; Additional Chief Secretaries (ACS)-Development
of Provinces; and the Chairman of WAPDA. The Borrower has established the Federal Irrigation and
Drainage Cell (FIDC) in MW&P, which will be the Federal Secretariat for the project with responsibility to
the Borrower and cofinanciers for policy and institutional reforms under the project and for the functioning
of the FPSC. Working level coordination would be provided through an NDP Working Group which
would include representatives of WAPDA and Provinces. A table showing the organization structure for
the project is given in Annex 8.
4.6    WAPDA and Provincial Coordination: Implementation guidance, supervision and monitoring
of WAPDA's activities would be the responsibility of the Member and Managing Director (Water). At the
Provincial level, implementation guidance, supervision, and monitoring would be provided through
Provincial Project Steering Committees (PPSCs) chaired by the ACS-Development of the Planning and
Development (P&D) Department or Chairman of the P&D Board, of the Province. Day-to-day project
coordination, including on preparation and submission of appropriate documentation and follow-up to
agreed actions with the Borrower, Provinces, donors, and PAs, would be through the NDP Coordination
Cell headed by an NDP Program Coordinator, which has been established and is functioning. The NDP
Coordination Cell is currently located in WAPDA. Additional provincial coordination would be provided
through four Provincial NDP Coordination Cells, each of which is headed by a Provincial NDP
Coordinator appointed by the ACS-Development/Chairman of the P&D Departnent/Board, with
responsibility for day-to-day coordination and supervision of the project in the Province. The coordination
arrangements would be reviewed in the light of implementation experience and would be modified as
necessary. Assurances were obtained at Negotiations that: (i) WAPDA would maintain for the duration of
the project an NDP Coordination Cell in WAPDA 's Water Wing with organization, terms of reference and
staff satisfactory to IDA; (ii) Each Province would maintain for the duration of the project, a Provincial
NDP Coordination Cell, with organization, terms of reference and staff satisfactory to IDA; and (iii) the
NDP Coordination Cells in WAPDA and Provinces would be headed by an NDP Program Coordinator
and Provincial NDP Coordinators respectively whose rank, qualifications, authority, and responsibilities
would be satisfactory to IDA.
4.7    Enhanced Supervision by the Borrower: In line with the decision taken during the 1995
Country Portfolio Performance Review (CPPR), the project would provide resources to support enhanced
project supervision by the Borrower, WAPDA, and Provinces. WAPDA has appointed a consortium of
firms as the NDP engineering, design and supervision consultants for the project to assist in project
implementation. The Borrower, WAPDA, or Provinces would, in addition, hire the services of specialists
in various fields and Panels of Experts (POEs) on their behalf from time to time to independently supervise



32
the project. Consultants, selected in consultation with IDA, appointed by the Borrower, WAPDA, or
Provinces to conduct independent supervision missions, would submit their deta[iled Supervision Reports
(or in the case of POEs, Reports of the POE) to the Borrower, WAPDA, or Province as the case may be,
with copies to IDA.
4.8    Financial Management, Accounts and Audit: The Borrower's and PAs' compliance with audit
covenants on on-going Bank-financed projects has been satisfactory, although there is still scope for
improvement particularly on the quality and timeliness of financial statements and audits. An 'Accounting
and Financial Management Handbook' developed during project preparation with assistance from a leading
national firm of Chartered Accountants would be the framework for the maintenance of accounting
records, preparation of financial statements, and financial management for the project. The Borrower,
WAPDA and Provinces would establish an NDP Financial Management and Information Systems (FMIS)
Unit with professionally qualified accountants (i.e. Chartered Accountants), management specialists, and
information systems professionals with responsibility for project accounting and financial management
including development and implementation of the necessary information systems. WAPDA would engage
a firm of Chartered Accountants/Management Consultants as FMIS Consultants to develop, implement and
maintain a uniform (standard) accounting, payment and financial information system for the project; and to
assist PAs improve their financial management, accounting, budgeting, cost control systems, and
management information systems (MIS) in all functional areas. Annual audits of expenditures and
Financial Statements42 for the project and for each PA would be performed by one or more nationally
reputed firm(s) of Chartered Accountants. The NDP Coordination Cell, in consultation with the Auditor
General of Pakistan and IDA, would appoint the independent auditor for the project. Since the services of
the audit firm would be financed from the proceeds of the Credit, IDA's procedures for appointment of
consultants will apply. Eligibility criteria for PAs includes appointment of a firm of Chartered Accountants
as Auditors and submission of unqualified audited financial statements. Each PA would, in consultation
with the Auditor General or the respective Accountants General and IDA, appoint a firm of Chartered
Accountants as Auditors. Assurances were obtained at Negotiations that: (i) WAPDA would engage FMIS
consultants with qualifications and terms of reference satisfactory to IDA by June 30, 1998; (ii) adequate
records and accounts to reflect, in accordance with sound accounting practices, the operations, resources
and expenditures in respect of the project would be maintained; and (iii) the NDP Coordination Cell would
submit the audit reports for the consolidatedfinancial statements of the project to IDA not later than six
months after the close of each fiscal year.
4.9    The New Portfolio Management Strategy: The proposed project woulcl be supervised under the
overall framework of the water sector portfolio management strategy; and would eventually become the
centerpiece of IDA's portfolio management program for projects in the water sector in Pakistan. IDA's
project design and portfolio management strategy emphasizes: (i) restructuring of problem projects; (ii)
MTRs for all projects, and linking disbursements thereafter to performance against previously agreed
targets, and agreement on subsequent performance targets; (iii) improving construction management
practices; (iv) annual CPPRs; (v) joint reviews/supervision with borrowers; (vil firmer management of
Loan/Credit Closing Dates; (vii) ensuring quality at entry through accelerated project start-up, project
launch workshops, and increased beneficiary participation; (viii) curtailing the role of the public sector and
expanding that of the private sector and NGOs in Bank-supported projects; (ix) eliminating policy
distortions; (x) improving the efficiency and productivity of irrigation and drainiage public expenditures;
(xi) streamlining and capacity building for key public institutions; (xii) using independent design and
supervision consultants to improve technical quality; (xiii) the use of Standard Bidding Documents
(SBDs); (xiv) strengthening sectoral linkages; (xv) requiring actions on key policy and institutional reforms
before projects are approved rather than relying on dated covenants; (xvi) requiring the borrower to take
up-front actions such as appointment of key staff, appointment of consultants, preparation of procurement
42     Maintained by the NDP FMIS unit in WAPDA, and including the Management Letters, Special Accounts
and SOEs.



33
plans and bidding documents, approval of planning documents, preparation of detailed designs etc. before
Negotiations/Board approval; and (xvii) thematic focus on supervision.
4.10    Supervision Plan: In accordance with the project's conceptual approach and IDA's strategy, IDA
would decentralize an increasing proportion of its responsibility for supervision of the project to its
Resident Mission in Pakistan (RMP) and a Resident World Bank Project Monitor43, beginning with
procurement review and routine supervision. The donors and the Borrower would jointly supervise the
project. IDA Supervision Missions would be held twice yearly in April and November. The April Mission
would focus on reaching agreement with the Borrower on the size and composition of the NDP PSDP, and
an Action Plan to improve the performance of the NDP project portfolio. The November Mission would
focus on field visits and implementation issues. The Borrower would appoint independent firms or
individual consultants, and POEs, in consultation with IDA, to supervise the project, including to appraise
and supervise individual Investment Projects, particularly complex or large Investment Projects (e.g.
RBOD, DG Khan, O&M Performance Contracts, or Evaporation Ponds) which are proposed to be financed
under the project. The Borrower would: (i) submit the Quarterly Progress Report of the Project within one
month of the end of each Quarter; (ii) prepare a MTR Report for the project, and review it with IDA not
later than December 31, 2000. The MTR would cover the first three years of project implementation and
include review of the functioning of the PIDAs, AWBs and FOs. The NDP donors would review their
financing commitment to the project based on the results of the performance of the project as reflected in
the MTR and its recommendations, and agreement with the Borrower on follow-up actions. In view of the
nature and scope of the project, its estimated annual supervision budget from IDA's regular budget (i.e.
excluding consultants funded from Trust Funds and the Credit) would be in the order of sixty (60) staff-
weeks per annum.
4.11    Project Launch Workshops: To facilitate project start-up, WAPDA (in collaboration with its
respective Divisions, Regions, and PRIs), and each PIDA (in collaboration with its prospective PAs such as
AWBs, FOs, PAD-OFWM, and NGOs) would hold a project launch workshop shortly after Credit signing.
The workshop would familiarize staff and officials of the PAs with the objectives and strategies of the
project in general, and the project components, implementation schedules, and institutional arrangement,
the project's approach to gender and poverty alleviation issues, procurement and disbursements
procedures, M&E aspects, eligibility criteria, Investment Project selection criteria, and requirements for
preparation and submission of Business Plans and financial statements and reporting.
4.12    Training Workshops: The FIDC in MW&P of the Borrower, the NDP Coordination Cell,
Provincial NDP Cells and each PIDA would organize training workshops for staff and managers of
prospective PAs on the project's requirements and procedures for procurement, disbursements, preparation
of Investment Projects, annual work programming, beneficiary participation, land acquisition and
resettlement planning, social assessments, and environmental assessments.
D. SOCIAL AND ENVIRONMENTAL ASPECTS
4.13    Social Design and Gender Issues: PAs would conduct Social Assessments (SAs) for all
Investment Projects and for FOs established under the project. SAs would be used to provide frameworks
for incorporating participation, gender, poverty focus, and social analysis into the design and delivery of
project interventions to beneficiaries particularly poor and women beneficiaries, and project-affected
persons (PAPs).  SAs would include: (a) identification of key stakeholders; (b) establishment of
frameworks for participation of stakeholders in project interventions; (c) ensuring that the project's design
and implementation strategies pay particular attention to gender and poverty alleviation considerations; (d)
ensuring that project objectives and incentives for change are acceptable to the range of people intended to
43     This assumes that Consultant Trust Funds would continue to be available to finance the costs of such
intensive supervisory services.



34
be benefited; (e) assessment of social impacts of interventions; (f) identification of methods of mitigation
of adverse impacts; (g) assessment of social risks; (h) identification of institutional arrangements for
participation and delivery of the intervention; and (i) formulation of plans to build institutional capacity.
4.14   Information, Education and Communication (IEC):  The NDP "oordination Cell would
prepare and update annually, in coordination with the Borrower and the Provinces, and implement, an IEC
Strategy for the project. A high standard in favor of information disclosure would be applied for all project
activities, including procurement, disbursements, EIAs, resettlement plans, Investment Project reports, and
main covenants. All PAs, working with NGOs and other community groups, would subscribe to the
project's policy and standards in favor of a high degree of public participation and disclosure of project
information to the public and the stakeholders in particular. This Staff Appraisal Report (SAR), the Credit
and Project Agreements, ELAs, and Resettlement Action Plans (RAPs) would be available to the public
through IDA's Public Information Center (PIC) and through WAPDA's NDP Coordination Cell shortly
after Board Approval. However, PAs would be entitled to levy reasonable cost recovery charges on the
recipients of the information.
4.15    Land Acquisition and Resettlement: Assurances were obtained at Negotiations that WAPDA
and Provinces would ensure that all land acquisition and involuntary displacement, and the subsequent
resettlement and rehabilitation associated with interventions of the project, are implemented in accordance
with the Framework for Land Acquisition and Resettlement (FLAR) which has already been agreed among
the Borrower, Provinces and IDA. The FLAR, inter alia, provides that: (i) involuntary resettlement would
be avoided or minimized wherever feasible through viable alternative designs; (ii) land acquisition and
resettlement activities, including compensation and/or rehabilitation, would be completed before
commencement of associated civil works; and would be a condition for award of civil works contracts
requiring land acquisition and resettlement; (iii) all Project Affected Persons (PAPs) would be compensated
and rehabilitated to improve or at least maintain their living standards and income earning capacity, and to
share in the project's benefits; (iv) entitlement packages would include compensation to improve standard
of living of PAPs and training, work opportunities for landless and poorer groups of PAPs; (v) an
Independent Monitor for Land Acquisition and Resettlement would supervise the implementation of the
FLAR. Submission of a land acquisition plan and RAP, and the allocation of a budget would be required
for all Investment Projects which involve land acquisition and resettlement and for works related to all
Investment Projects in the pipeline, including the RBOD. The Feasibility Report Consultants estimated
that about 3 acres of land would be acquired for each kilometer of off-farm surface drains rehabilitated or
built. The FLAR has estimated that about 9,500 acres of land would be required for the Investment Projects
to be implemented under the project. Current information indicates that except for a portion of the Right
Bank Outfall Drain (MNVD-Indus Link), there would be no displacement due to construction works. In
addition, all farmers whose lands may be acquired for the projects would benefit many times over through
the reclamation of their lands. By contrast to their land acquisition requirements, the Investment Projects
(ongoing and planned new) would reclaim an estimated 22,430 acres of currently abandoned land,
rehabilitate another estimated 162,850 acres of land, and provide drainage relief' over 5 million hectares.
The fielding of Social Organizers and the involvement of NGOs would also assist in providing fair and
timely compensation for the affected families and resettlement sites that are at least comparable with the
original sites.
4.16   Environmental Assessment: The Borrower formally submitted, and IDA has accepted, the
DSEA as the Environmental Impact Assessment (EIA) for the project in January 1996. Based on the
DSEA, the project has been assigned Environmental Category B. All Investment Projects would conform
to strict environmental criteria, especially those dealing with effluent disposal; and no Investment Project
would be included unless there is a disposal strategy for the drainage effluent. Assurances were obtained
at Negotiations that WAPDA and Provinces would ensure that: (i) an Initial Environmental Scoping (IES)
is carried out for all Investment Projects; and (ii) full EIAs are prepared, to the satisfaction of IDA, for all
Investment Projects whose IES indicate that they would have significant environmental impacts. Such
projects would only be approved for funding under the project if their EIA indicate that the adverse
environmental impacts would be substantially mitigated or avoided. Environmnental Action Plans (EAPs)



35
pursuant to the EIA would be agreed with IDA before such Investment Projects are approved for funding
under the project. The RBOD Project and some of the other expected Investment Project proposals would
require an EIA. WAPDA's Environmental Cell and Provincial EPAs would have the lead role in
preparation, review, approval, and implementation of environmental aspects of the project including EIAs,
EMPs, environmental audits, and IEC campaigns for the activities to be carried out by WAPDA and
Provinces respectively.
4.17   Environmental Management Plan (EMP): The project's EMP includes the following activities:
(a) environmental coordination; (b) establishment of an enviromnental baseline based on a sound spatial
framework identifying agro-ecological zones; (c) environmental assessments; (d) identification of
ameliorative measures; (e) design and implementation of those measures and of an Environmental
Monitoring and Evaluation System (EMES); (f) design and implementation of the project's Environmental
Awareness Program; (g) design and implementation of the project's Wetlands Management Plan (WMP);
and (h) expansion, upgrading and operation of the Environmental Data Management System (EDMIS).
Assurances were obtained at Negotiations that WAPDA and Provinces would implement the EMPfor the
project as a whole dealing with the cumulative basin-wide environmental aspects and implications; and
environmental mitigation or enhancement measures indicated in the EIAs for individual Investment
Projects, to the satisfaction of IDA.
4.18    Environmental Monitoring and Audit: Independent enviromnental audits will be carried out
during project implementation to review and evaluate its environmental performance. All environmental
reports related to the project will be made available to interested organizations and persons in Pakistan and
abroad on the basis of full cost recovery. Assurances were obtained at Negotiations that WAPDA and
Provinces would: (i) appoint, by December 31, 1997, WAPDA's Environmental Cell and the Provincial
EPA respectively, or an internationally recognized environmental specialist acceptable to IDA, as the
Environmental Auditor for their activities under the project during the period of implementation; (iH) cause
the Environmental Auditors to issue their independent "Annual Environmental Audit Report" no later than
six months after the close of each fiscal year beginning from FY97/984; (iii) publish a summary of
important audit findings and recommendations in the project's progress reports; and (iv) implement the
recommendations of the Environmental Audit Reports.
4.19   Wetlands Management Plan (WMP): The WMP will focus on critical wetland areas affected by
the IBIS. It will include mitigating measures (where applicable), specifications and recommendations on
irrigation and drainage planning, and measures to improve wetlands protection. To avoid the risk that
some wetlands may be reclaimed through drainage (especially in the riverine areas), assurances were
obtained at Negotiations that WAPDA and Provinces would: (a) take measures to ensure that in
constructing field drains, wetlands are adequately protected"; and (b) provide maps and information on
reclaimed areas, including the land use pattern for reclaimed lands and the date of reclamation. Any
proposal to drain Sindh Riverine areas, areas between the main drains and the coast, flood plains, or lake
shores, would be subject to ElAs, in accordance with Pakistan's environmental protection laws and Bank
policy, prior to seeking IDA's approval for the Investment Project.
4.20    Environment &  Social Design Consultants: Pre-implementation experience to date has
demonstrated the critical need for up-front preparation of proper EIAs, EMPs, beneficiary participation,
land acquisition and compensation, RAPs, information, education and communication strategies, and SAs
(including involvement of NGOs and incorporating gender in design and implementation). Consultants
would be engaged to assist a diverse set of clients including WAPDA's Regional Offices, Environment
and Social Cells, Provincial NDP Cells, EPAs, PIDAs, AWBs, FOs and Community Organizations; and to
build the institutional capacities of the PAs on these issues (including through establishment or
44     Year ending June 30, 1998.
45     For examnple, by restricting field drains to existing cultivated areas which do not pose serious threats to
wetlands.



36
strengthening of the relevant Cell in the PA, training and deputation of staff). The Consultants would
actively collaborate with Provincial agencies (e.g. Provincial EPAs, Divisional &. District Administration),
NGOs, community groups, the media, and other outside agencies. The Consultants' contracts would make
provisions for the primary consultants to sub-contract specific aspects of their terms of reference to NGOs,
Community Organizations, and other collaborating entities.  The consultants' teams would include
specialists in environment, gender, NGOs, resettlement, IEC, and social development. Assurances were
obtained at Negotiations that WAPDA would engage environment and social design consultants with
qualifications and terms of reference satisfactory to IDA by June 30, 1998.
5. BENEFITS, JUSTIFICATION AND RISKS
A. BENEFITS AND POVERTY IMPACTS
5.1    Summary: The project's most significant direct benefit would be to tenant farmers, smallholders,
and tail-enders in the form of increased efficiency of service delivery, improved availability of irrigation
and drainage supplies, and reduced cost. The project's principal short term benefits will come from the
physical drainage and irrigation improvements and better O&M resulting in more effective drainage, which
in turn will result in an improved environment and increased output of agricultural produce. This will have
a direct and positive impact on poorly drained land that falls within the command of each Investment
Project, regardless of the socioeconomic characteristics of farmers cultivating these lands. Estimates
indicate that farmers benefiting from the drainage improvements can expect to increase their income by up
to 30 percent, depending on the original condition of waterlogging and salinity and on the agro-ecological
conditions. The project's principal medium- and long-term benefits (particularly from the institutional
reforms in Provinces) will come from  improved drainage and irrigation-related policies, improved
efficiency of public expenditures on drainage O&M, improved capability in the public sector to plan and
implement water sector projects and in the private sector to undertake drainage-related investments,
improved and more equitable availability of irrigation supplies and drainage facilities, improved efficiency,
and (potentially) lower cost of delivery of irrigation and drainage services as a iresult of decentralization
and participation by beneficiaries. These benefits will potentially be far greater than those from the
physical investments. All of these will benefit the smallholder farmers and tenants in particular, and
environment and the agriculture sector as a whole; and enhance sustainability of the land and water
resource base. A detailed analysis of project benefits and costs is given in Annex 9).
5.2    Production Benefits: The project would have two important impacts. Drainage installation
would lower the groundwater table and reduce soil salinity, thereby leading to significant improvements in
yields. The project also addresses a long-term issue: without the project, future yie-lds would progressively
decline from their current levels. Therefore, the project would have two additional benefits: (a) avoidance
of further yield (production) losses, which would result if the groundwater and salinity problems were to
persist; and (b) productivity increases resulting from reduced waterlogging/salinity problems.  The
"without" project scenario is one of a continuing trend of substantial losses in agricultural production, and
ultimately further environmental deterioration and increased rural poverty for farmers. The increasing
levels of salinity and waterlogging would reduce yields to almost zero. Large areas of crop land would
completely go out of production in the "without" project scenario. Productivity increase is, by far, the
most important of the two benefit subsets. Insofar as improved drainage may have positive effects on
agricultural technologies or cultural practices which are not included in the above estimate, the benefits are
understated. Based on the identified and projected pipeline of Investment Projects, the project is expected
to benefit many farmers directly, and the entire irrigated agriculture sector indirectly, by reclaiming
abandoned land, rehabilitating existing lands, and potentially improving drainag;e in about 13.5 million
acres. Productivity is estimated to improve over a period of about five years by about 2 percent for the
entire IBIS command areas, and by about 20 percent for tail-end farmers. Tail-end farmers would also
realize additional benefits from a reduction in costs of production and improved availability of water
through the activities of FOs. Specific interventions of the project such as lining watercourses and



37
rehabilitation of canals would also increase the amount of fresh water that reaches the tail-end of
watercourses in SGW areas.
5.3    Positive Poverty Impacts: Areas which are affected by high levels of waterlogging and salinity
tend to have the lowest incomes, high unemployment, and lawlessness in Pakistan. Hence waterlogging
and salinity problems have a poverty and equity dimension too. They affect the rural poor (especially
those at tail-ends of watercourses and minors) and landless tenants most severely. By helping to raise
agricultural output, farm incomes, providing additional employment opportunities in those areas, the
project will help alleviate rural poverty and improve the socio-economic prerequisites for law and order.
The selection criteria for Investment Projects are designed to skew the benefits in favor of tenant farmers,
smallholders, and tail-enders. Specifically, Investment Projects would, to the extent possible, be designed
to benefit small farmers, women, and poorest one-third of the income strata of the members of the farming
community. This would be demonstrated through a Social Assessment for the Investment Project. The
institutional reforms, particularly the formation of FOs, are expected to increase the transparency and
equity of water allocation and use.
5.4    Other Positive Impacts: The project's interventions would reduce the O&M expenditures for
social infrastructure such as roads, power distribution, public housing, etc. The project would also provide
appropriate spill-over effects on non-farm growth through backward and forward linkages, including
increasing employment opportunities in the non-farm sector Estimates from research findings indicate that
every one percent increase in per capita farm growth would provide a 1.5 percent increase in per capita
non-farm growth.
B. FINANCIAL AND ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
5.5    Relation to the Economy (Macroeconomic Impact): An assessment of the macroeconomic
sustainability of the proposed project indicates that the planned public investment (with the project) in the
sector as a share of projected GDP remains very close to historical trends. The share of the drainage sector
investment, including the project, in the PSDP would also remain close to historical levels, partly because
many of the Investment Projects to be funded under the project are ongoing Investment Projects that are
already in the PSDP, and the beneficiaries also share in investment costs. An illustrative analysis is
presented in Annex 9, Table IA. This analysis was done by comparing key macroeconomic indicators on a
set of macroeconomic projections with and without the project, using data on GDP, PSDP, investment
costs, and the financing plan for the project.  This analysis assumed a somewhat slow pace of
macroeconomic and structural reforms, but no backtracking, (as assumed in the CAS for Pakistan) as the
macroeconomic framework (Base Case) for the project; and therefore used projections of the GDP growth,
inflation rate, exchange rate of the rupee to major currencies, and level of budgetary PSDP indicated in the
"muddle through" scenario in the CAS. The project's requirement of counterpart funds is within the
capacity of the Borrower and its Provinces for financing and implementation. Its ADP counterpart
requirements will be highest (at Rs. 2171 million or about US$42.5 million) in the fifth year of
implementation (FYOI/02)4.
5.6    Fiscal Savings: The project's most significant direct benefit to the Provinces is expected to be in
the form of fiscal savings, if Provinces implement the project's institutional reform program as envisaged.
The savings would come from transfer of responsibility for O&M from Provinces to farmers. Annual
savings to Provinces is estimated at Rs. 2,519 million ($62 million) per year, representing about 16% of
O&M budget, by the year of completion. These savings are based on the following conservative estimates
and assumptions: (i) transition of 1,500 FGW tubewells from the public sector to farmers' groups and FOs

million (US$43.1 million); FY01/02: Rs. 2171 million (US$42.5 million); FY02103: Rs. 2120 million (US$39.8
million); and FY03/04: Rs. 1719 million (US$31.2 million); and FY04/05: Rs. 606 million (US$10.7 million).

would save the Provincial Governments operating expenses of Rs. 616 million per year; (ii) transfer of
responsibility for O&M of on-farmn surface drains of less than 15 cusecs to FOs would save the Provincial
Governments Rs. 14 million per year in operating expenses and salaries of beldars (daily rated temporary
workers); (iii) replacing public sector SGW tubewells with private sector (farner-managed) tile drains
would save the Provinces Rs. 124 million per year; and (iv) transfer of responsibility from Provinces to
FOs for O&M of irrigation and drainage operations at the minor/distributary level could potentially save
Provinces Rs. 1765 million per year47. The detailed analysis of the project's impact on the recurrent budget
is given in Annex 9, Table lB. The above estimates are in addition to projected savings of Rs. 883 million
in annual subsidies by the Province of Punjab under the parallel Punjab Privfate Sector Groundwater
Development Project (Cr. 2901 - PAK), in which 4,233 SCARP tubewells would be transitioned. In
addition, other savings (not quantified) would include reduced maintenance costs for housing, roads and
other social infrastructure, and public health. Efficiency gains from the improved efficiency, autonomy
and hard budget constraint of PIDAs and AWBs (which are both expected to achieve full financial self-
sustainability of O&M costs in 10 years) would provide additional fiscal savings.
5.7     Tax Revenues: The available evidence from recently completed drainage interventions under
LBOD (notably Nawabshah component) have demonstrated that the tax revenue and overall level of
economic activities generated by drainage investments can be very substantial. The tax revenue potential
from the project is based on the cropped area which is expected to benefit from the project, and assumes no
change in the existing composite tax structure for major crops. The area affected by the project would
depend on the intervention assumed. The Feasibility Study has estimated that the area which is likely to
benefit from the project's interventions could be as high as 13.5 million acres. If this estimate is realized,
the potential (not projected) yield from tax revenues to Provinces would vary from a low of Rs. 1,200
million to as much as Rs. 3,000 million" per annum.
5.8     Financial Returns to the Borrower and its Provinces: Based on the above analysis of Fiscal
Savings and Tax Revenues, the Internal Rate of Return (IRR) to the Borrower and Provinces (considering
the Borrower's and Provinces' investments in the project, and the financial returns in the form of fiscal
savings and tax revenues) is estimated at about 11.4% (nominal terms). This reflects, in large part, the
impact of subsidies for capital works. The payback to the Borrower and its Provinces from the project is
about 5.5 years after project completion.
5.9     Balance of Payments: The project's direct benefit to the Borrower and the economy at large is
likely to be felt in its impact on the country's balance of payments (BOP) position in terms of foreign
exchange saved or earned. To the extent that the projected incremental net earnings from Investment
Projects49 strengthen the country's BOP position, they would contribute to the Borrower's capacity to
service the debt associated with the project.

5.10    Investment Project Analysis: The selection criteria requires that Investment Projects should have
a minimum economic rate of return (ERR), excluding non-quantifiable benefits, of 15 percent. The various
types of Investment Projects which would be supported under the project have had historically (in
Pakistan) high ERRs (20%-50%)5. Studies of completed projects in Pakistan also show that higher ERRs
are achieved under program rather than project approach for the same type of investments. Drainage
projects in other countries also show high rates of return5'. The economic analysis for the potential
Investment Projects in the pipeline indicate that substantial number of these projects, covering various
types of interventions, would meet the ERR selection criteria. These are detailed in Annex 9. The ERRs
of the various types of Investment Projects also span a wide range. These reflect variations in cropping
patterns and yield levels, the degree of surface flooding and its frequency, the extent of waterlogging, and
the available irrigation supplies.
5.11    Farm  Incomes: Illustrative farm budget analysis, conducted for sample farms and for various
types of Investment Projects, shows that the project would increase farm incomes by 6% to 128%
depending on the agro-climatic zone and the current situation; and that beneficiaries would be able to pay
their share of capital and O&M costs associated with the project's investments and institutional reforms,
while still continuing to realize substantial increases in their net incomes.
5.12    Cost Sharing: Arrangements for cost sharing are an integral part of the project's design. They
aim to increase the contribution of farmers for capital costs, and to progressively ensure that all O&M costs
are recovered52. The project's design for farmners' contributions to capital costs is based on the principle of
substantial up-front cost sharing, which has been demonstrated through experience to be superior to back-
end cost recovery. Past history has shown that back-end cost recovery for capital costs tends not to work
in reality, and moreover unduly involves non-financial institutions (notably PAD-OFWMs) into credit
activities for which they are not equipped or organized; and that that farmers' contributions are higher, and
administrative costs lower, under up-front cost sharing. Unlike in previous projects where the Borrower or
Provinces constructed and maintained irrigation and drainage projects without prior approval of farrners,
the farners will largely be the sponsors of on-farm drainage and irrigation projects under NDP. They will
also be responsible for post-construction O&M costs of this infrastructure. The Borrower and all Provinces
have recently introduced an agricultural wealth tax and agricultural income taxes to tax agricultural
incomes. The Borrower has also agreed that it would formulate proposals to ensure that recovery of capital
costs is linked to benefits derived from the project especially for large landholders e.g.: by updating
Produce Index Units (PIUs) in the Investment Project areas based on baseline and post-completion
cadastral surveys. The cost sharing arrangements for various types of Investment Projects would be
stipulated in the Participation Agreements between Provinces and PIDAs, AWBs and FOs; and would be as
detailed in the table below:
