ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
1.     Introduction: The framework for   economic analysis links the project's interventions
(institutional reforms, institutional, research and sector planning, and drainage investments) to anticipated
impacts and benefits. The analysis establishes linkages between the project and country and sector
strategies and includes quantitative and qualitative analyses of fiscal impacts, portfolio management,
poverty, environmental impacts, institutional analysis, and Investment Projects.
A.     SECTOR ANALYSIS AND RATIONALE FOR PUBLIC SECTOR ROLE
2.     Sectoral Analysis/Reviews: Research and IDA experience suggests that next to sound economic
policy framework, the basis for sound investment decisions and implementation performance is a sound
sectoral analysis, particularly public expenditure reviews (PERs). The project is based on a number of
sectoral analyses, three of which are most important: (i) the Borrower's Water Sector Investment Planning
Study (WSIPS, 1993); (ii) the Borrower's Drainage Sector Environmental Assessment (DSEA, 1993); and
(iii) the Bank's Irrigation and Drainage Issues and Options paper (1994).
3.     Public Expenditure Reviews: IDA, jointly with ADB and OECF has conducted an Annual
Review of the Public Sector Development Program (PSDP) since 1992. IDA has conducted an Annual
Country Poryfolio Performance Review (CPPR) with the Borrower, WAPDA, and Provinces since 1992.
The water sector has featured prominently in the annual reviews of the PSDP and CPPRs. The Feasibility
Study for the project included a detailed review of the Public Expenditure Program for Drainage. The
results of the Bank's study of "Fiscal Decentralization in Pakistan" were incorporated in the design of the
project during Appraisal. By recalculating the ERRs of projects which were originally contained in the
PSDPs of the Borrower and Provinces, the NDP Feasibility Study Consultants were able to recommend a
different mix of projects in the PSDPs, saving the Government millions of dollars from the combined
PSDP of the Borrower and Provinces. It is expected that the project will provide the basis, mechanism, and
resources around which the continuous evaluation of the PSDP for irrigation and drainage would be
evaluated annually, and high priority public sector investments financed.
4.     Private Sector Counterfactual: The public expenditure programs which would be financed
under the project have been identified after several rigorous reviews of the aspects of drainage and
irrigation capital and recurrent expenditures which the private sector can or should provide. The reviews
and sector analysis cited above concluded that the private sector should be responsible for substantial
investment costs, and almost all recurrent costs of on-farm drainage, lining of watercourses in SGW areas,
FGW tubewells, and tertiary drainage (subdrains). Off-farm drainage, however, is largely a public good
and regarded to be within the public sector domain, because the costs of inadequate drainage, costs of
providing remedy in the form of drainage relief, and benefits of drainage are borne largely by the public
(though mostly the farming community) at large. The cost recovery and sharing proposals also take into
account incidence of public investment and private or public gains. The project is designed to make the
transition to the public-private roles described above.
5.     The Policy Environment: Economic theory indicates that rates of return to investments such as
the project would be higher if they are implemented in a policy environment which has fewer distortions.
Pakistan has implemented many policy reforms in the past decade which have helped to improve the policy
environment for successful implementation of irrigation and drainage projects.
6.     Water Rights: Water rights exist in Pakistan on an informal basis. There is a growing concern in
Pakistan about increased and inefficient allocation and use of water resources. Attention is therefore being
focused on the use of water rights in water allocation. Water rights (through price signals) could better
allocate water and provide incentives for more efficient water use in agriculture, industry, and urban areas.
Tradable water-use rights that are separate from land may be established. Past use of water could be a
basis for allocation. Once established, water rights can be officially registered. Since a major objective of



97                                        ANNEX 9
Page 2 of 18
NDP is to reduce the drainable surplus inter alia through improving efficiency in water use and improving
canal delivery systems, a study of water rights would be inplemented under the project.
7.     Experience of countries such as Chile that have encouraged and practiced water markets in water
management has important implications for countries facing water scarcity and are considering introducing
water markets: there are significant gains from trade which can realized by fostering water markets;
transferable water-use rights or water rights, separate from land, are essential for water markets (these
rights can be stipulated by volume or by percentage of river or canal flow); great care should be exercised
in the initial allocation of water-use rights among users in order to make sure that most of the rights are not
captured by a few individuals; technology, such as adjustable gates, and institutional arrangements that
encourage the formation of active water users' associations can substantially redluce transaction costs and
facilitate market trading; the presence of privately held water rights does not necessarily make it more
difficult to reach environmental water quality objectives (water quality regulations need to be established
and enforced irrespective of the water allocation system); within a decentralized system of water resource
management, there is a continuing role for water management authorities in enforcing water rights and
resolving conflicts; and where irrigation systems have been built with public funds, there is an important
question concerning how much, if any, of the economic surplus generated by water trades should
governments try to capture. For a country like Pakistan with long rivers and extensive return flows, the
issue of water rights for users of return flows would have to be addressed in their water code.
B. EXPECTED BENEFITS
8.     Introduction: The project is an integral part of Pakistan's National Drainage Program (NDP),
which is anchored within the country's medium-term public expenditure plan. It focuses on the core
priority drainage investments. Its principal short term benefits will come from improvements in the
physical drainage and more effective operating drainage, which would leadl to increased output of
agricultural produce. It would also have a direct and positive impact on drained farm lands, regardless of
the socioeconomic characteristics of farmers cultivating these lands. It is estimated that farmers benefiting
from the drainage improvements can expect an increase in their net farm income of up to 30 percent,
depending on the local drainage and the agro-ecological conditions.
9.     The principal medium- and long-term  benefits will be improved drainage  and irrigation-
related policies, improved efficiency of public expenditures on drainage O&M, iimproved capability in the
public sector to plan and implement water sector projects and in the private sector to undertake drainage-
related investments, all of which will benefit the environment and the agricultural sector as a whole. These
benefits will potentially be far greater than those from the physical drainage investments.
Fiscal Impacts
10.    The project's fiscal impacts will be reflected in the capital and recurTent budgets at both the
Federal and Provincial levels. The project is also expected to have an impact on investment portfolio
management. These impacts are discussed below.
II.    Development Budget Impacts: Analysis was conducted to address the issue of fiscal impact of
the project, in part because concern has been expressed regarding the project's size. Given the fungibility
of money, IDA could in effect be financing, at the margin, a different project than the one appraised.
Accordingly, it was also important to be satisfied that the sectoral and overall investment program was
appropriate. The fiscal analysis draws on results of recent public expenditure reviews.
12.    The Borrower's efforts to reduce its fiscal deficit has resulted in a progressively tightening
squeeze on the PSDP. At the same time, improving disbursement performance and higher lending by some
of the major donors imply rising budgetary requirements to provide for the needs of foreign assistance
program. There is an emerging inconsistency between the tightening budget and the growing foreign
assistance program. Adverse effects of this inconsistency are likely to be maniiested in under-budgeting



98                                       ANNEX 9
Page 3 of 18
the requirements of donor assisted projects and programs; and a tendency for the foreign aided component
of the PSDP to exceed the budget target (since some disbursements are independent of counterpart funds or
budget restrictions), weakening fiscal programming.
13.    Macroeconomic Impact (Relation to the Economy): An assessment of the macroeconomic
sustainability of the proposed project was done by comparing key macroeconomic indicators on a set of
macroeconomic projections with and without NDP, using data on GDP, PSDP, investment costs, and the
financing plan for the project. A somewhat slower pace of macroeconomic and structural reforms (but no
backtracking) as assumed in the Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) for Pakistan, is used as the
macroeconomic framework for the NDP. Thus, the GDP growth, inflation rate, exchange rate of the rupee
to major currencies, and level of PSDP are determined by the "muddle through" scenario in the CAS. The
illustrative analysis is presented in Table IA (given at the end of this Annex)'. The share of the drainage
sector investment, including NDP, in the PSDP would remain close to historical levels, because many of
the projects to be funded under NDP are ongoing projects that are already in the PSDP and the
beneficiaries also share in investment costs. Planned public investment (with NDP) in the sector as a share
of projected GDP also remains very close to historical trends.
14.    The 1995/96 PSDP review found that allocations for a large number of high priority donor
assisted projects fell short of their funding requirements (both in terms of projected foreign aid and local
counterpart funding). Measures are being taken to alleviate the problem. The Borrower has agreed to
continue to formulate a core development program and a three-year rolling investment plan in consultation
with IDA and other donors; protect the allocations of the core projects/programs from possible cuts
necessitated by fiscal considerations; continue to review the pace of implementation during the year of the
projects for which the PSDP allocations of counterpart funds fall short of requirements and, if needed,
provide additional funds from the savings of slow moving projects; and regularly review the project
portfolio in consultation with IDA and other donors, and close or restructure under-perforrning and/or low
priority projects.
15.    The proposed project is consistent with the PSDP framework agreed between the Borrower and
IDA. Both parties agree that there is a need to increase investment in drainage to deal with accelerating
waterlogging and salinity. IDA has agreed with GOP on the size and composition of a three-year rolling
PSDP for the water sector for 1996-97 to 1998-99. Drainage is accorded high priority in the current rolling
program in which NDP is included. Under the project, IDA will agree with the Borrower (Planning
Commission) on the size and composition of the rolling prograrn for the project by April of each year.
16.    Counterpart Funding Requirements: The project's requirement of counterpart funds is within
the bounds of the Government's capacity for financing and implementation. Its Annual Development Plan
(ADP) counterpart requirements will be highest (at Rs. 2171 million or about US$42.5 million) in the fifth
year of implementation (FYO1/02)2. Participation by beneficiaries in capital investment costs of the project
would also reduce pressure on government counterpart funding. For example, for tile, surface and
subsurface drainage investments, and improvement of watercourses and irrigation systems, the private
sector would contribute about $31.1 million through labor and land.
17.    Recurrent Budget Impacts: The project's expected impact on the recurrent budgets of the
Provinces is presented in Table IB (given at the end of this Annex). The table represents the "base case"
The illustrative analysis was done using macroeconomic projections given in the latest CAS document. It is
expected that the results of the analysis would not change with updates of macroeconomic projections and NDP
financing plan.

scenario which assumes that the institutional reform program is implemented according to plan. The
project's most significant direct benefit to the Provinces is expected to be in the iform of fiscal savings. The
savings would come from transfer of responsibility for O&M from Provincial Governments to farmers.
Annual savings to Provinces is estimated at Rs. 2,519 million ($62 million), representing about 16% of
O&M budget, by the year of completion. These savings are based on the following conservative estimates
and assumptions: (i) transition of 1,500 FGW Tubewells from the public sector to farmers' groups and
FOs would save the Provinces between Rs. 616 million per year in operating expenses; (ii) transfer of
responsibility for O&M of on-farm surface drains of less than 15 cusecs to FOs would save the Provinces
Rs. 14 million per year in operating expenses and salaries of beldars (daily rated temporary workers); (iii)
replacing public sector SGW Tubewells with private sector (farner-managed) tile drains would save the
Provinces Rs. 124 million per year; and (iv) transfer of responsibility from Provinces to FOs for O&M of
irrigation and drainage operations at the minor/distributary level could potentially save the Provinces Rs.
1765 million per year3. The above estimates are in addition to projected savings of Rs. 883 million in
annual subsidies by the Province of Punjab under the parallel Punjab Private Sector Groundwater
Development Project, in which 4,233 SCARP tubewells would be transitioned. In addition, other savings
(not quantified) would include reduced maintenance costs for housing, roads and other social
infrastructure, and public health. Efficiency gains from the improved efficiency, autonomy and hard
budget constraint of PIDAs and AWBs (which are both expected to achieve full financial self-sustainability
of O&M costs in 10 years) would provide additional fiscal savings.
18.    Revenue Impacts: Another source of fiscal benefits from the project is tax revenue. The tax
revenue potential from the project is based on the expected benefiting cropped area, and assumes no
change in the existing composite tax structure for major crops. The applicable tax rates from the Left Bank
Outfall Drain (LBOD) Command, for which data is most readily available from a recent study4, are used as
the basis for the illustrative analysis. Not counting Abiana, the most recent combined rates for Land
Revenue, Ushr Rates (Productivity Tax), and Local Fund (County Tax) per acre in the LBOD Command
are Rs. 132 for wheat, Rs. 140 for cotton, Rs. 87 for rice, Rs. 227 for sugarcane, Rs. 137 for oilseeds, and
Rs. 90 for vegetables5. The area affected by the project would depend on the intervention assumed. The
area which is likely to benefit from the project's interventions could be as high as 13.5 million acres. If this
estimate is realized, the potential ( not projected) yield from tax revenues to Provinces would vary from a
low of Rs. 1200 million (assuming combined rates of Rs. 87 per acre) to as much as Rs. 3000 million
(assuming combined rates of Rs. 227 per acre).
19.    The above analysis assumes that everything goes according to plan. However, in the course of
implementation it is likely that the project would encounter problems in some of its fiscally important
components. For example, the progress in transferring O&M responsibility from Provincial Governments
to farmers may be slower than anticipated and tax revenues from benefiting cropped areas may be smaller.
This would constitute a "low case" scenario under which the fiscal savings and tax revenues would be less
than anticipated.
20.     Balance of Payments: The project's direct benefit to the Borrower and the economy at large is
likely to be felt in its impact on the country's balance of payments (BOP) position in ternns of foreign
exchange saved or earned. To the extent that the projected incremental net earnings from Investment

Projects6 strengthen the country's BOP position, they would contribute to the Borrower's capacity to

21.     Financial Returns to the Borrower and its Provinces: Based on the above analysis of Fiscal
Savings and Tax Revenues, the IRR to the Borrower and Provinces (considering the Borrower's and
Provinces' investments in the project, and the financial returns in the form of fiscal savings and tax
revenues) is estimated at about 11.4% (nominal terms). This reflects, in large part, the impact of subsidies
for capital works. The payback to the Borrower and its Provinces from the project is about 5.5 years after
project completion.
22.     Portfolio Management: Drainage projects in Pakistan have typically been inadequately prepared,
programmed, implemented, and not well integrated with Five Year or the Annual Development Programs
(ADP). Moreover, the selection of projects has not always been rational or transparent. Allocation of
development funds is on the basis of fixed provincial shares and not according to drainage needs. The
result is that the portfolio of drainage projects is characterized by implementation delays, too many
incomplete projects, inadequate financing (especially of counterpart funds), poor quality of construction,
low rates of return at entry, and cost overruns. The Feasibility Study for the project showed that the
"throwforward," a term used by the Planning Commission to describe the value of projects which are
carried from one Five Year or Annual Development Plan into the next, from the Seventh Five Year Plan
(1989-93) into the Eighth Five Year Plan (1993-98) rose to a record of Rs. 54.7 billion. Current estimate
of the "throwforward" into the Ninth Five Year Plan (1998-2003) indicate that without considering any
new investments, it would be Rs. 15.7 billion. However, considering new projects and the project's
financing for the last three years of the Eighth Plan, the Feasibility Study estimated that the
"throwforward" would be reduced to only Rs. 5.2 billion, mainly due to availability of funding as a result
of NDP.
23.     Successful implementation of the project should result in a substantial increase in public
investment and aid flows into major irrigation and drainage infrastructure during the Ninth Five Year Plan
period, notably for the proposed National Surface Drainage System (NSDS). This increase in public
investment is critical for the future viability of irrigated agriculture in the Indus Basin Irrigation System. It
would reduce the size of "throwforward", enable timely completion of projects due to increased availability
of funds, and improve project quality due to better project preparation made possible by adoption of more
stringent selection criteria set up in the context of the project. Moreover, the project and future projects
such as NSDS would link the portfolio of drainage projects to a coherent long-term strategy for disposal of
effluent outside the basin.
Economic Efficiency Impacts
24.     Potential Efficiency Gains: The various interventions supported by the project are expected to
lead to efficiency gains. Drainage research and sector planning would lead to efficiency gains by
enhancing the technical foundations of drainage, and facilitating long-term sector and project planning.
Institutional reforms would bring about efficiency gains by strengthening the capacity and improving the
efficiency of key drainage institutions, and ensuring that the project design is within the implementation
capacity of existing institutions. Reforms in WAPDA (Water Wing), establishment of PIDAs, AWBs, and
FOs should enhance the effectiveness of their service delivery. Investments under the project would
generate efficiency gains in water delivery and drainage. Abiana collection would be expected to improve
under the proposed assessment and collection system.
6       The illustrative analysis of incremental net economic earnings on six Investment Project districts in Sindh
Province for which detailed farm budget analysis was done may be to demonstrate of the BOP impact. The analysis
indicates that incremental net earnings would turn positive from year 2001 at about Rs. 2.6 billion ($65 million), and
increase gradually thereafter (assuming no switching in cropping patterns and no market limits).

Page 6 of 18
Employment Impacts
25.    Direct Impacts: The project is expected to generate many direct emrployment benefits to the
economy. Effective implementation of the Project Coordination and Supervision Component would
provide opportunities for redeployment of surplus staff in MW&P, WAPDA's W7ater Wing, PIDs (in their
new form as PIDAs, AWBs and FOs), and PAD-OFWMs to gainful employment in the implementation of
a sector-wide project, the first of its kind and scope since the IBRWP. Without the project, many of these
staff would be effectively redundant (i.e. technically unemployed) in their present employment.
Considering the direct financial costs, and indirect efficiency costs to the institutions and the economy of
carrying these redundant staff, the economic benefits accruing from their gainful employment under the
project are substantial, though no attempt has been made to quantify them. Of course, many of these
employees would again become redundant when the implementation of the project is completed, unless the
levels of investment and employment are maintained through follow-up projects. To protect against the risk
of over-manning which is associated with the project's direct incremental staff requirements, WAPDA's
Water Wing, PIDAs, AWBs and FOs will not employ additional staff on account of the project or any
other project for the duration of the project, without the prior agreement of the Borrower or the Provinces,
as the case may be, and only on the basis of sound financing plans.
26.    When fully implemented i.e. after project completion, the Institutional Reforms Component could
likely result in a net reduction of the number of employees in WAPDA and PIDs. The phased withdrawal
of WAPDA's Water Wing from construction of on-farm drainage such as surface drains, tile drains and
SGW tubewells, would provide an opportunity or the needed flexibility for WAPDA to reduce its low-
skilled casual (i.e. daily rated) workers in the Regional Offices. Reorganization, automation, process
improvement, and mechanization of some of WAPDA's functions which are currently over-manned or
managed using outdated methods (specifically accounting, monitoring, planning and investigation) could
result in substantial reduction of surplus staff and costs. On the other hand, expansion of the Water Wing's
other functions (e.g. O&M of inter-Provincial drainage; vigorous pursuit of the construction of inter-
Provincial drainage, establishment of a vibrant WSIS, establishment of the Social Cell and other capacity
building initiatives, and active management of the IBIS water resources) should[ compensate for much of
the expected redundancies from the retrenchments alluded above. Some of the staff can be redeployed to
the new functions. PIDAs, AWBs and FOs may eventually be compelled by new financial self-sufficiency
requirements to mechanize many of the functions which are carried out by beldars and chaukidars. The use
of private contractors for O&M (e.g. through O&M Performance Contracts) would also likely result in
reduction of disguised unemployment. The most significant redundancy effects though are expected to
occur with the turnover of distributaries and minors to FOs i.e. at the EXEN level; because it is at this level
that PIDs employ the majority of their low-skilled staff (notably, clerks, beldars and chaukidars); many of
whom would no longer be required if farmers take over their functions.
27.    The most significant employment impacts of the project are expected to arise from the Investment
Component, after completion. Short term direct employment impacts would accrue from construction
activities. After completion, the investment component would reclaim or preserve abandoned agricultural
farmlands, and rehabilitate existing land, which would otherwise ultimately go out of production due to
waterlogging and salinity. Based on the average land holding by Province, the estimated benefit is
employment for about 16,660 farmers; in addition to reducing disguised unemployment for thousands
more farmers whose lands are currently marginally productive, and would be restored through the project's
investment activities.
Poverty Impacts
28.    Positive Poverty Impacts: Areas which are affected by high levels of waterlogging and salinity
tend to have the lowest incomes, high unemployment, and lawlessness in Pakistan. Hence these problems
have a poverty and equity dimension too. They affect the rural poor (especially those at tail-ends of
watercourses and minors) and landless tenants most severely. The project would sassist in poverty reduction
by increasing agricultural output (food and cash crops). Since agriculture is among the most effective tools



102                                         ANNEX 9
Page 7 of 18
for poverty alleviation, by helping to raise agricultural output, farm incomes, and providing additional
employment opportunities in those areas, the project will help alleviate rural poverty and improve the
socio-economic prerequisites for law and order. The selection criteria for Investment Projects are designed
to skew the benefits in favor of tenant farmers, smallholders, and tail-enders. Specifically, Investment
Projects would, to the extent possible, be designed to benefit small farmers, women, and poorest one-third
of the income strata of the members of the farming community. This would be demonstrated through a
Social Assessment for the Investment Project. The institutional reforms, particularly the formation of FOs,
are expected to increase the transparency and equity of water allocation and use. The poor would also
benefit from flood protection provided by the project.
29.     The project is designed to facilitate reduction and evacuation of the drainable surplus so as to
increase agricultural production that is broadly based and provide appropriate spillover effects on non-farm
growth through backward and forward linkages. Thus, the project would address the multifaceted problem
of poverty alleviation by increasing employment opportunities in the non-farm sector and increasing
productivity of marginal farmers and landless tenants. For every I percent increase in per capita farm
growth, it is estimated that the project would provide a 1.5 percent increase in per capita non-farm growth
in the year 200 1'.
30.    Income Distribution Impacts:  While the project's water management investmnents would
improve water resource allocation efficiencies between owners of land at the head and tail of canals, it is
likely that agricultural production benefits would still be skewed towards large land owners. However,
benefits to large land owners would likely encourage domestic resource mobilization in rural areas, which
would lead to benefits to the poor in terms of additional farm and non-farm employment opportunities.
There is a reasonable probability that the drainage investments would benefit the rural elite and landlords
to a greater extent than the rural poor, thus exacerbating rural income inequality. To address this risk,
Investment Project selection criteria would favor areas and beneficiaries who are in the bottom one-third
income strata of the farming community. In addition, the requirement that beneficiaries contribute to the
cost of installing drains in their areas through labor would likely select-out the rural elite. However, the
rural elite may simply hire day laborers to do the job. The project's actual and potential impacts on rural
income, poverty, and income distribution would be monitored closely during the first phase of
implementation, and necessary adjustments would be made, based on the results of the MTR.
Environmental Impacts
31.    The project's adverse environmental impacts are expected to result from disposal of subsurface
drainage effluent, leaching of salts from agricultural lands, habitat changes in waterlogged and saline lands,
and resettlement. However, these adverse impacts are expected to be very narrowly focused and would be
completely offset by broad-based positive impacts. The positive effects of the environment would include
improvements on waterlogged lands, soil salinity, groundwater quality, flood protection, and afforestation.
The project's expected social and environmental impacts are summarized in the Table below.

increase in per capita non-farrn production growth from a I percent in per capita farm production growth.

Expected Beneflts from the Research and Sector Planning Component
32.    The Research Subcomponent: The research subcomponent is designed to improve effectiveness
of irrigation and drainage systems in the country. The research programs will test: various new technologies
under Pakistani conditions. The principal benefits expected from the identified drainage research program
are expected to be the following: (i) improved technologies and reduced costs of investment and O&M of
drainage, arising from design and maintenance procedures for surface drains; (ii) improved hydraulic
designs for surface drains; (iii) reduced cost and improved efficacy of collaborative (community) tile
drainage; (iv) recommendations for institutional frameworks to improve sustainability and productivity of
irrigated agriculture in Pakistan; (v) designs and recommendations for resource rnonitoring using GIS on a
sectoral approach; (vi) breakthroughs in reuse and disposal of drainage water, which would substantially
reduce the drainable surplus and increase the supply of irrigation supplies available; (vii) information for
planning and policy regarding the impact of tile drainage on land and water management; and (viii) designs



104                                       ANNEX 9
Page 9 of 18
of hydraulic and salinity models to assist AWBs and FOs to manage their hydraulic systems more
efficiently.

34.    Historical benefits from drainage research by IWASRI (one of the PRIs) in Pakistan illustrate the
up-side potential benefits which can be expected from the proposed drainage research subcomponent. The
benefits of improved drainage design can be gleaned from the Fourth Drainage Project. During the design
and construction of the Fourth Drainage Project, important reductions in cost, compared with first designs,
were realized as a result of integrating the findings of concurrent research which was undertaken on one of
the project sites (sump). The length of subsurface drains was reduced from 1,600 miles to 500 miles, and
the number of sumps from 250 to 79; with a reduction in project nominal costs from Rs. 700 million to Rs.
300 million despite the effects of inflation associated with an implementation period of 11 years.
Application of groundwater models to drainage design enabled the identification of spatially varying
drainage needs, which led to the abandonment of the uniform spacing of subsurface drains that had
previously been planned/assumed for the design of the component.
35.    The benefits of research on design criteria for drainage can be inferred from the recent work of
IWASRI; which shows that the drainage coefficient for tile drainage projects which were designed before
1990 was overly conservative; and has therefore been reduced in more recent projects. The reduction in
the drainage coefficient has not only greatly reduced the capital cost of drainage projects, but also the
recurrent costs and the quantity of drainage effluent for disposal, thereby reducing the adverse
environmental impact associated with disposal requirements of drainage effluent on the downstream users,
and other effects of tile drainage. As a result of these research findings, the drainage coefficient has been
reduced from 2.7 mm/d under Fordwah Eastern Sadiqia (in 1990), to 1.8 mm/d in planning for Khushab
SCARP; and the drainage coefficient for Fordwah Eastern Sadiqia has recently been reduced to 1.5 mm/d



105                                        ANNEX 9
Page 10 of 18
following the discussions which emanated from IWASRI's research. This shows that designers of drainage
projects in Pakistan have now gained confidence that a design with a lower drainage coefficient can
concurrently meet the objectives of watertable control, reducing project caipital and recurrent costs
substantially. The proposed subcomponent on drainage research under the project will support further
research to better determine the drainage coefficient.
36.    The benefits of Pakistan's recent research on interceptor drains are equally significant. Seepage
from canals is one of the major contributors to waterlogging. Interceptor drains to recover this seepage
have been constructed at a few sites in Pakistan; and were planned for Fordwah Eastern Sadiqia (South)
Project, and under LBOD. The estimated cost of interceptor drains in Fordwah Eastern Sadiqia (South) is
Rs. 500 million; while the cost in LBOD is Rs. 768 million. Research fndings from IWASRI after these
projects had been approved indicated that interceptors should be used selectively, and not as a standard
technique for recovering seepage from canals, because the effectiveness of the interceptors was too low
(30%, which implied that to recover 0.5 cusec net, 2.7 cusec has to be pumped); and would lead to
excessive O&M costs.
37.    Drainage research has also contributed to the reduction of costs for drain envelopes. In Pakistan,
gravel is traditionally used as drain envelope; whereas there is a worldwide trend to use synthetic materials.
IWASRI has started laboratory and field tests to establish applicability of synthetic materials as drain
envelope. Test lines have been laid in Fourth Drainage Project, and are scheduled for Fordwah Eastern
Sadiqia Project. Switching from gravel to synthetic envelopes has already resulted in capital cost savings
as high as 40% in the Fourth Drainage Project with the same level of drainage collection and efficiency.
38.    The Sector Planning Subcomponent: Empirical evidence shows that sound sector framework is
essential to achieving development impact of projects. Sector studies are essential tools for guiding policy
formulation and implementation. Such studies would include policy, strategy, planning and feasibility
studies. The expected benefits of the sector planning subcomponent are not easily quantifiable. However,
potential benefits which are expected from each of the main areas of intervention under the planning
subcomponent are summarized in the following paragraphs.
39.    Policy Studies: These are expected to result in an improved policy environment for public and
private investment in irrigation and drainage. A partial list of the potential benefits which are expected
from each of the main policy studies proposed or agreed to be carried out under the project are summarized
below. The National Water Policy would (i) enable investments in major inter-Provincial water resources
development programs such as reservoirs and trunk drains; (ii) codify the role of the Borrower in the water
sector; and (iii) allow official recognition of water as an economic good. The study of water markets and
water rights in AWBs and in selected FO pilot areas could eventually result in the: (i) establishment of a
National Register of Water Rights; (ii) legalization of water trading up to the AWB level, possibly between
AWBs; and (iii) greater willingness to price surface water at more realistic (market based) levels. The
study on gypsum marketing and distribution would be expected to result in a greater: (i) willingness of the
Borrower and Provinces to deregulate gypsum marketing, and to abolish the erratic subsidy on gypsum,
which appears to be a major deterrent to development of an effective private sector-led market in gypsum;
(ii) greater availability and use of gypsum for treatment of sodicity; and (iii) in the long run, increased
utilization of gypsum and the associated reduction in land areas lost to sodicity. The study on promoting
private investment in drainage would be expected to provide the basis for: (i) policies to encourage private
sector investment in drainage, particularly from communities in partnership with private contractors; (ii)
reduction in subsidies for drainage; (iii) more rapid drainage and reclamation of waterlogged and saline
lands, as private investment substitutes public investment; (iv) policies and programs which result in more
focused targeting of public investment in drainage to areas where the public good is clearly dominant, and
away from areas where private gains are substantial; and (v) improvements in capital and O&M cost
recovery resulting from clearly defmed policies in that regard. The study on regulation of exploitation of
FGW is expected to recommend: (i) policies to regulate and promote optimal exploitation and permit
recharging of FGW aquifers; (ii) clearer delineation of the roles of Federal and Provincial agencies in



106                                       ANNEX 9
Page II of 18
regulation of FGW exploitation; and (iii) reduction of salt-water intrusion in FGW aquifers arising from
unregulated over-exploitation of FGW aquifers.
40.    Planning Studies: The benefits which are expected from the agreed or identified planning studies
are as follows: (a) the Irrigation and Drainage Atlas of Pakistan is expected to result in substantial
improvement in (i) planning for irrigation and drainage; and (ii) utilization of water resources; (b) the
Long-Term Financing Options for Drainage study is expected to result in recommendations and policies
which would encourage private investment in drainage, as well as policies to facilitate public sector
financing for drainage e.g. developing capital markets for financing drainage; (c) the study of Revenue
Prospects for WAPDA 's Water Wing would lay the groundwork for policy and institutional reforms which
would enable the Water Wing to become financially self-sustaining in the long run, for example by selling
its services to the Power Wing; (d) the study of Revenue Options for PIDAs, A WBs, and FOs would enable
them to identify prospects and mechanisms for them to increase the buoyancy of their revenue sources,
from Abiana and from other sources; (e) the study of Efficacy of Past Drainage Investments by the
Planning Commission is expected to result in recommendations for changes in the Borrower's policies and
priorities for financing drainage, including PC- 1 guidelines for project preparation, selection, and
implementation; and (f) the Preparation of the Ninth Five Year Plan (1998-2003) is expected to facilitate
the preparation of a high quality contribution from the Water Wing of the Planning Commission into the
Ninth Five Year Plan.
41.    Feasibility Studies: The following are among the feasibility studies for future potential projects
which would be carried out under the project: (i) National Surface Drainage System (NSDS; (ii) LBOD
Stage II; (iii) Preparation of NDP 11; and (iv) Feasibility Studies for Hydraulic Modeling of Canal
Commands for A WBs and FOs. These feasibility studies would be in addition to the modular feasibility
studies and business plans for PIDAs, AWBs, and FOs that would be financed under the project as part of
the institutional reform program. The benefits which are expected from the agreed or identified feasibility
studies are: (i) a strong pipeline of better prepared high priority projects; (ii) consensus on implementing
major inter-Provincial drainage projects; and (iii) attraction of donor financing for drainage programs. Of
course, the real benefits of the pipeline of high priority national drainage projects whose feasibility studies
would be financed under the project would accrue when they are implemented and operated satisfactorily.
Expected Benefits from the Institutional Reforms Component
42.    Institutional Reforms in WAPDA's Water Wing: Expected benefits from institutional reforms
in WAPDA's Water Wing include improved performance of the institution, for example, coordinated
management and regulation of water resources at the basin level, including related land and water quality
issues and monitoring of environmental impacts.
43.    Institutional Reforms in PIDs: Substantial benefits are expected to come from  the
decentralization and management transfer of irrigation and drainage services from PIDs to PIDAs, AWBs,
and FOs. Since PIDAs, AWBs, and FOs will be independent revenue generating and spending agencies
(the latter owned by farmers themselves), they will have incentives to minimize their expenditures and
recover full costs from beneficiaries. This will reduce the fiscal dependence of the irrigation and drainage
system on Provinces for funding O&M. This will work more effectively if it is accompanied by improved
access to water through the establishment and granting of tradable water rights, and a clear demarcation of
duties between the new irrigation and drainage agencies. These reforms will in effect introduce quasi-
market incentives into the management of the irrigation system. Significant benefits can be expected to
accrue from the legalization and/or establishment of water rights in FOs, which is among the policy
reforms that would be implemented under the project. Where farms are small (or for administrative
convenience), water rights could in some instances be allocated to FOs and WUAs. According to a recent
survey by WAPDA, active water markets (trading or purchasing of water) exist in 70 percent of



107                                          ANNEX 9
Page 12 of 18
watercourses studied by WAPDA'. According to research, establishment of tradable water rights could
make important contributions in improving the efficiency, equity, and sustainability of water use9. Well-
defined tradable rights would formalize and secure the existing water rights held by users; economize on
transaction costs; and induce water users to consider the full opportunity costs of water. If well-defined
water rights are established in FOs, the entities and individual farmers can be expected to invest in water-
saving technologies knowing that they would benefit from the investment.
Expected Benefits from the Investment Component
44.     Introduction: Research evidence shows that rehabilitation projects have highly variable net
benefits, and that it is important to be selective in the choice of Investment Projects to be financed. Large-
scale, capital intensive rehabilitation which seeks to remake the entire system is prone to rent-seeking,
delays, and cost overruns that characterize construction of new systems. Therefore, investments in
improved irrigation and drainage facilities should ideally be closely linked to establishment of tradable
water rights. For the purpose of assessing the economic rate of return of the project, the direct
quantifiable benefits from rehabilitation and improvement of the public sector surface drains and
construction of on-farm drainage works would be considered. These interventions will improve drainage
conditions, resulting in higher crop yields and cropping intensity.
45.     Production Benefits:  The proposed project would have two important impacts. Drainage
installation would lower the groundwater table and reduce soil salinity, thereby leading to rapidly improved
yields and lower production cost. The project also addresses a long-term issue: vvithout the project, future
yields would progressively decline from their current levels. The project would have two additional
benefits: (a) the avoidance of further yield (production) losses, which would result if the groundwater and
salinity problems were to persist; and (b) the productivity increase resulting from reducing
waterlogging/salinity problems. The "without" project situation would lead to a substantial loss in
agricultural production and an environmental disaster and great suffering for farmers. The rising of the
deep saline watertable would reduce yields to almost zero once it reaches the crop root zone. Large areas
of crop land would completely go out of production in the "without" project scenario. Productivity
increase is the most imnportant of the two benefit subsets. The sum of the benefits represents the "with and
without" project situation, rather than the "before and after" situation. Insofar as improved drainage may
have positive effects on agricultural technologies or cultural practices, the benefits are understated.
46.     The project is expected to benefit many farmers directly, and the entire irrigated sector indirectly,
by reclaiming abandoned land, rehabilitating existing lands, and improving drainage in about 13.5 million
acres. Agricultural benefits would come from increased crop production and employment as a result of
reduction in watertable depth and soil salinity, and better operations and maintenance of irrigation system
through private sector involvement and improved performance of public institutions in the water sector as a
result of institutional strengthening. Cropped area, cropping intensity, and crop yields are expected to
increase as a result of the project. Cropping patterns are also expected to change due to reduced soil
salinity, waterlogging, and flooding. Crops that are less tolerant to salinity and waterlogging can be grown
in the "with project" situation. Production would increase over the "without project" situation due to both
(i) the direct effects of decreased saline intrusions and (ii) improved drainage. The annual incremental
production expected from the project's investment component is summarized below.
47.     New Investments and On-Farm  Drainage and O&M:   Based on the currently available
information on the pipeline of on-going or new drainage projects which the project would finance, the
8       WAPDA: Trading of Canal and Tubewell Water for Irrigation Purposes; P'lanning and Investigations
Publication No. 358, 1990.
9       The following description is based on the findings of research work done by Mark Rosengrant (IFPRI) and
Hans Binswanger (World Bank), in Markets in Tradable Water Rights; World Development, Vol. 22, No. 11 pp. 1613-
1625, 1994.



108                                      ANNEX 9
Page 13 of 18
project's new investment subcomponent would provide new or additional on- and off-farm drainage
facilities for: (i) on-going locally funded projects; (ii) transition of FGW tubewells; (iii) new surface drains;
(iv) intercepting drains; and (v) community tertiary and tile drains in the LBOD Command (Mirpurkhas),
D.G. Khan Integrated Irrigation and Drainage, Fourth Drainage, SCARP Mardan, and East Khairpur.
Additional benefits would accrue from new investments which have been identified but not prepared or
appraised, and some which have not yet been identified. The benefits of new investments are assumed to
mirror but be higher than those for rehabilitation and modernization (see para below), although the benefit-
cost ratio would be more favorable for rehabilitation and remodeling.
48.    Rehabilitation &  Modernization of Off-Farm  Drainage: A number of surface drains are
proposed for rehabilitation and remodeling. Projections for agricultural production without surface drain
investments in the selected areas are that yields would gradually decrease and over a thirty year period be
reduced by 50 percent. Costs of production would gradually increase as planting and sowing, harvesting,
and fertilizer continue to grow as a percentage of production inputs. Surface drains are expected to reduce
the number of days the land is waterlogged and decrease salinity and thus reclaim cultivable command
area, improve planting and harvesting efficiency, and decrease costs of production including planting and
sowing, harvesting and fertilizer.
49.    Rehabilitation &  Modernization of Water Management: The component is expected to
provide canal commands with improved telecommunications and equipment. Rehabilitation of the canal
commands is expected to improve water carrying capacity of drains and reduce crop losses from flooding.
It will also significantly increase water deliveries to tail-end farmers. Modernizing canal commands with
improved telecommunications and equipment would have a positive impact on the distribution of water
and provide for better water control. Improved distribution of water will affect agricultural production in
the entire cultivable command area and improve productivity.  The intervention would also increase
productivity and decrease maintenance costs of production for tail-end farmers, who will now receive a
steady and reliable source of water. Agricultural productivity is expected to start increasing from the year
after completion of construction works. It is estimated that productivity would improve by 2 percent for
the entire command area and by an additional 20 percent for tail-end farmers. Tail-end farmers would also
realize additional benefits from a 1.5 percent reduction in costs of production. Productivity and costs are
expected to remain steady in future years after the initial impact. It is estimated that rehabilitation would
increase productivity and cropping intensities by 2 percent as a result of reduced crop damage from
breaches in canals and flooding, and an additional 5 percent for tail-end farmers, who would observe a
significant increase in surface water deliveries.
50.    Lining Watercourses in Saline Groundwater Areas:  The primary objective of lining
watercourses is to increase agricultural production through effective use of irrigation water saved by
renovation of watercourses and application of improved on-farm water management practices. It is
expected, for example, that 750 watercourses would be lined in saline groundwater (SGW) areas around
Nara Canal Command and Khairpur East; the projections for agricultural production without lining
watercourses in saline areas of Nara and Khairpur in the Sanghar and Khairpur Districts are that yields
would remain constant and cost of production would increase gradually due to rising costs of planting,
harvesting, etc. It is estimated that lining watercourses would have a positive impact on the distribution of
water at the watercourse level and would affect agricultural production by 11 percent in Sanghar and
Khairpur. It is also anticipated that the intervention would provide for saving water conveyance losses by
5% and 10% in lined and unlined watercourses, respectively. Benefits on agricultural productivity are
expected to occur immediately the year after construction. Productivity and costs are expected to remain
steady in future years after the initial impact.
51.    Potential Production Losses: The investment component of the project is expected to lead to
some loss in agricultural production since some agricultural land will be acquired for project purposes.
The NDP Feasibility Study Consultants estimated that about 1.21 hectares of land will be acquired for each
kilometer of off-farm surface drains rehabilitated or built. On that basis, it is estimated that a total of 8,696
hectares of land are likely to be acquired. Current average net income in the project areas is estimated at



109                                            ANNEX 9
Page 14 of IS
Rs. 17,670 per hectare per year. Thus average annual losses from agricultural income from land acquired
for project purposes would be about Rs. 153.7 million. This loss would be more than offset by expected
benefits.
Estimation Of Economic Rates Of Return And Net Present Values
52.     The selection criteria requires that Investment Projects should have a minimum economic rate of
return (ERR), excluding non-quantifiable benefits, of 15 percent. The various types of Investment Projects
which would be supported under the project have had historically high ERRs (200/%-50%) in Pakistan'0.
Studies of completed projects in Pakistan also show that higher ERRs are achieved under program rather
than project approach for the same type of investments. Drainage projects in other countries also show
high rates of return". The economic analysis for the potential Investment Projects in the pipeline (given in
the Table below) indicate that substantial number of these projects, covering various types of interventions,
would meet the ERR selection criteria. The ERRs of the various types of Investrment Projects also span a
wide range. These reflect variations in cropping patterns and yield levels, the degree of surface flooding
and its frequency, the extent of waterlogging, and the available irrigation supplies.

I      Input indicators measure the quantity (and sometimes also quality) of resources provided for project activities. Process indicators measure the   0
effectiveness and timeliness with which a project's inputs are being procured, used or deployed to generate outputs.
2      Output indicators measure the quantity (and sometimes also quality) of goods and services that have been created or provided through the use of
inputs.
3      Outcome indicators measure the quantity (and sometimes also quality) of direct results that have been achieved through the provision of project goods
and services. Impact indicators measure the degree to which wider sector /project objectives are being achieved through the direct outcomes of project
activities.
